Preface 
This dissertation was written as a completion of the Double Nordic master in Environmental Engineering 
with the study track Residual Resources. The eNviro5Tech programme offers the MSc degree, which is a 
part of Nordic5Tech. The MSc was conducted at the Technical University of Denmark at the Department of 
Environmental Engineering and the Norwegian University of Science and Technology at the Industrial 
Ecology Programme. 
The thesis, called “Limitations and quality in plastic recycling“, was carried out from September 2014 to 
February 2015. The thesis represents 30 ETCS credits. A pre-study was conducted in the spring of 2014, 
called “MFA of recyclable plastic flows – focus on Denmark and Norway and the limitations to recycling“, 
as a special course of five ETCS points.  
Supervisors from DTU were Senior Researcher Anders Damgaard, Associate Professor Thomas Fruergaard 
Astrup and PhD Student Kostyantyn Pivnenko. Professor Helge Brattebø from NTNU co-supervised. 
Collaboration with the consultant company Rambøll involved Senior Consultant Dorte Hvid-Jacobsen and 
Regional Director Lone A. Clowes. I would like to thank all of my supervisors for valuable input, discussions 
and support. 
A special thanks to Anders Egede Daugaard, Associate Professor at the  Department of Chemical and 
Biochemical Engineering at DTU for helpful conversations on plastic chemistry. Additionally, I greatly 
appreciate rewarding conversations with and interest from;

==PAGE:5==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
iv 
 
Abstract 
The overconsumption of the Earth’s resources has increased the focus on waste as a resource. Plastic 
consumption has grown immensely since its introduction, while it is currently the least recycled material 
flow in Europe. Moreover, if the Earth’s environment is to benefit from plastic recycling, the quality of 
the recycled material needs to increase so that secondary plastic can replace virgin plastic. Policy 
instruments have an increasing focus on circular economy, but knowledge regarding quality in plastic 
recycling is missing for supporting the realisation of such type of economy. The most challenging plastic 
fraction stems from households due to its heterogeneous nature. 
The general objective of this thesis is to investigate how it is technically feasible to recycle household 
plastic waste in Denmark into high quality secondary raw material. This objective is aligned with the 
national long-term goal of closing the plastic recycling loop. Seven specific objectives are also defined, 
hereunder mapping the technical limitations, quantifying quality and assessing solutions to increase 
quality in recycling. The ultimate goal is to formulate recommendations for municipalities based on the 
knowledge obtained. 
The theoretical knowledge was obtained through literature reviews and interviews with stakeholders 
across the Danish plastic value chain. The method was used to value quality from a technical perspective 
was developed iteratively over the project period. A number of plastic grades were evaluated on their 
potential market coverage with recycled material. This was used to make a hierarchy of plastic qualities. 
It was found that a quality definition in legislation, not present at the moment, is necessary to steer 
improvements in quality of plastic recycling. The analysis showed that the quantitative household plastic 
recycling targets suggested in the European Commission’s Circular Economy Package are not reachable 
within the proposed timeframe in Denmark. The technical limitations in the upgrading process were found 
to depend on the contamination of the input material. Four contamination categories are of importance: 
polymer cross contamination, additives, non-plastic impurities, and degradation. There are great 
knowledge gaps on these contaminations and their impact on plastic properties, i.e. the quality. 
Quantification of quality was possible from an evaluation of the contamination and the amount of the 
household plastic market that could be covered by recycled material from household plastic waste. It was 
found that there is a great potential to increase high quality plastic recycling by avoiding and/or treating 
all four contaminations. Improvements in the whole value chain are necessary in order to increase quality. 
Still, possible changes in the value chain have both positive and negative environmental consequences, 
which need further investigation. Municipalities should consider including only recyclable plastics in the 
segregation schemes, excluding certain plastics that are detrimental to recycling like food-contaminated 
packaging. The latter was revealed as the lowest quality by the hierarchy.

Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen

Sammenfatning 
Overforbruget af jordens ressourcer har øget fokus på affald som en ressource. Siden introduktionen af 
plast er forbruget steget enormt, og det er i øjeblikket den materialestrøm i Europa, der genanvendes 
mindst. Hvis jordens miljø skal drage fordel af plastgenanvendelse, skal kvaliteten af det nyttiggjorte 
materiale øges, således at sekundær plast kan erstatte jomfrueligt plast, dvs. nyproduceret. Der er et 
stigende fokus på cirkulær økonomi i politiske beslutninger, men viden om kvalitet i plastgenanvendelse 
mangler for at støtte realiseringen af en sådan form for økonomi inden for plast. Den mest udfordrende 
del af plasten stammer fra husholdninger på grund af dens heterogene karakter. 
Det overordnede formål med denne kandidatafhandling er at undersøge, hvordan man teknisk kan 
genanvende plastaffald fra husholdningerne i Danmark til et højkvalitets råmateriale. Dette formål er på 
linje med det nationale langsigtede mål om at lukke plastkredsløbet. Syv delmål er også defineret, 
herunder at kortlægge de tekniske begrænsninger, kvantificere kvalitet og vurdere løsninger til at øge 
kvaliteten i genanvendelsen. Endnu et delmål er at formulere anbefalinger til kommunerne baseret på den 
opnåede viden. 
Den teoretiske viden blev opnået ved gennemgang af litteratur og interviews med aktører i hele den 
danske plastværdikæde. Metoden til at værdisætte kvalitet fra et teknisk perspektiv er blevet udviklet og 
evalueret gentagne gange (iterativ proces) i løbet af projektperioden. En række plastkvaliteter er blevet 
vurderet på deres potentiale for at dække markedet med genanvendt materiale. Dette blev anvendt til at 
lave et hierarki over plastkvaliteter. 
Det blev konstateret, at en definition af kvalitet i lovgivningen, som ikke er til stede i øjeblikket, er 
nødvendigt for at styre kvalitetsforbedringer i plastgenanvendelse. Analysen viste, at de kvantitative 
genanvendelsesmål for husstandenes plastprodukter som er foreslået i Europa-Kommissionens cirkulære 
økonomi-pakke ikke kan nås i Danmark inden for den foreslåede tidsramme. De tekniske begrænsninger i 
opgraderingsprocessen fandtes at afhænge af forureningsgraden af det indkomne materiale. Fire 
forureningskategorier er af betydning: polymer krydsforurening, tilsætningsstoffer, ikke-plast urenheder 
og nedbrydning. Der er stor mangel på viden omkring forureninger og deres indvirkning på de plastiske 
egenskaber, dvs. kvaliteten. Kvantificering af kvalitet var muligt ud fra en vurdering af forureningen samt 
andelen af husstandsplastmarkedet der kunne dækkes af genanvendt materiale fra husholdningernes 
plastaffald. Det blev konstateret, at der er et stort potentiale for at øge højkvalitetsplastgenanvendelse 
ved at undgå og/eller forbedre alle fire forureningstyper. Forbedringer i hele værdikæden er nødvendige 
for at øge kvaliteten. Der er behov for yderligere undersøgelser af de miljømæssige konsekvenser af de 
mulige ændringer i værdikæden, da det blev indikeret at disse kunne være både positive og negative. 
Kommunerne bør overveje kun at medtage genanvendelig plast i sorteringsvejledningerne og undtage 
bestemte plasttyper, der er til skade for genanvendelsen, f.eks. mad-forurenet emballage. Sidstnævnte 
blev afsløret som værende den laveste kvalitet i hierarkiet.

Page 1 of 110 
1. Introduction 
Increasing resource efficiency, closing material loops, creating green growth and introducing circular 
economy are buzzwords which have recently been in the political and media focus both in Denmark and 
Europe. The European Commission’s “Circular Economy Package” presented in September 2014 and later 
dropped for further discussion by the Commission and Parliament (DAKOFA, 2015a, 2014a; Messenger, 
2014) was a step towards a more resource efficient society. The concept of “circular economy” was 
developed by the Ellen MacAuthur Foundation (Ellen MacArthur Foundation, 2012) from the thoughts of 
among others Boulding (Boulding, 1966). The biological circular concept found in nature, where nothing is 
wasted, should be transferred to the technical sphere. Waste needs to be seen as a resource, so a circular 
material flow can be achieved. This means that the management of the obsolete products should already 
be considered from their design phase (Ellen MacArthur Foundation, 2012). Other concepts like Frosch and 
Gallopoulos’s “industrial ecology” (Frosch and Gallopoulos, 1989) and McDonough and Braungart’s “cradle 
to cradle” (Braungart and McDonoug, 2002) are similar in their thinking. 
Plastic is one of the most problematic waste streams in this regard, as it is currently furthest from being a 
circular material flow with increasing production, low recycling rates and high environmental burdens. 
Plastic is still a relative new material and the production growth have been immense, 9% annually since 
the 1950’ies (PlasticsEurope, 2013), but only recently recycling activities have started. Recycling is one 
option for keeping plastic in the loop, nevertheless plastic is the least recycled material fraction, both in 
absolute and relative terms, when compared to paper & cardboard, Waste of Electrical and Electronic 
Equipment [WEEE], glass, concrete, iron & steel, aluminium, copper and other metals in Europe (European 
Environment Agency, 2006). The amount of plastic recycling has grown by 10% annually in the last couple 
of years mainly due to legislation and environmental awareness (Hopewell et al., 2009; Ignatyev et al., 
2014). The increasing focus on plastic recycling can also be seen on the growing number of patents in the 
last decade within the field (Ignatyev et al., 2014). However, despite the progresses, knowledge and 
solutions in the field are still missing. 
Keeping plastic in the economy (closed material loop) as long as possible is also of importance for reducing 
fossil resources consumption and mitigating climate change. Currently plastic contributes with 5% of all 
industrial carbon emissions (Allwood et al., 2012). Such large contribution is explained firstly by the fact 
that 4% of all globally produced oil and gas is consumed in the plastic production process (Hopewell et al., 
2009). Secondly, because 4% of the world’s oil-derived energy is used in the plastic manufacture (EuPC 
2009 in Gent, 2011; Hopewell et al., 2009). Plastic management is also of interest due to the need for 
handling an increasing plastic waste generation caused by a growing demand of plastic products. The 
latter mainly obeys to an increasing population, rising affluence and replacement of traditional materials 
with plastic (Ignatyev et al., 2014). 
It is nevertheless important to understand that recycling for the sake of recycling is not always beneficial 
from a resource and environmental perspective - low quality recycling can be worse than other waste 
treatment alternatives, spread hazardous substances and generate rebound effects by increasing the 
overall consumption. Keeping plastic in the material loop would require higher qualities than observed 
today for the secondary material being able to replace virgin plastic in similar products and to go through 
another recycling cycle. An increasing amount of the recycled plastic, both on a Danish and European 
scale, is shipped to the East Asian economies, where it is used mainly in low quality products (Tønning and 
Ottosen, 2005; Velis, 2014; Velis and Brunner, 2013). In this regard the Chinese government has now taken 
actions to limit the amount of low quality secondary materials streaming into the country 
(Letsrecycle.com, 2015). Furthermore, another reason for desiring a better quality is the increasing 
amount of low quality recycled material on the market (supply), which is likely to cover the market for 
low quality products within a few years or decade (demand), i.e. making a surplus of material. This could 
==PAGE:16==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 2 of 110 
consequently decrease prices of the low quality materials, which have a limited range of application 
possibilities; hence, the market cannot expand much more compared to today. Knowledge about 
limitations and quality in recycling is missing, and need to be investigated further on order to increase 
quality. 
Legislation regarding plastic waste is numerous, both waste, environmental and chemical legislation are 
relevant. The Circular Economy Package included redefined recycling targets and calculation method. The 
targets proposed for plastic packaging recycling were 45% in 2020 and 60% in 2025, and the recycling 
efficiency calculation method was changed; from 1) measuring the amount recycled material as the 
collected amount, to 2) measuring the amount of input material to the recycling facility (with no more 
than 2% non-plastic contamination) (European Commission, 2014a).  
Frameworks like circular economy have inspired the newly released Danish resource strategy. One of its 
milestones for 2020 is high quality recycling (Justesen and Nielsen, 2014), but the pathway to get there is 
unknown. Prioritizing initiatives to improve recycling can be hard for individual stakeholders, as changes 
in one part of the value chain will have consequences elsewhere. Personal experience from a seminar with 
the Danish EPA and municipalities showed that the municipalities do not have resources to take well-
documented decisions regarding the new regulation on rigid plastic collection (DAKOFA and Miljøstyrelsen, 
2014). Current focus is only on quantities. In a Danish context, according to The Danish Plastic Federation, 
Denmark recycles only 10.5% of household plastic packaging today – the lowest rate in Europe (Wittrup, 
2014). Post-consumer plastic waste is a more difficult fraction compared to industrial plastic, because it is 
more heterogeneous and has greater contamination, hence the economic and hazardous risks are greater 
(La Mantia, 2002; Villanueva et al., 2010). Another explanation for the low recycling rate could be the 
high energy efficiency in Danish incineration. Furthermore, plastic waste has a higher energy content 
compared with average municipal solid waste [MSW], hence plastic recycling of MSW, apart from soft drink 
plastic bottles, has only recently been introduced in Denmark. The Danish government has recently set 
targets of increased recycling of plastic from residual waste, following the EU targets. The Danish Plastics 
Federation claims that it will be difficult to reach the newly proposed plastic packaging targets (Wittrup, 
2014).  
However, as mentioned earlier, recycling for the sake of recycling is not always technically or 
environmentally justifiable. Environmental comparisons between incineration and recycling more often 
favour incineration in Denmark compared to many other geographical locations due to the efficient 
incineration plants and low sorting efficiencies, which is an overlooked issue in LCAs (Merrild et al., 2012; 
Michaud et al., 2010; Rigamonti et al., 2014). Michaud and co-workers (2010) argue that the substitution 
of virgin materials, i.e. arguably the quality of the recycled product, is very determining for the outcome 
of a LCA. The substitution coefficient is however often not considered, i.e. favouring recycling which in 
theory could be disfavoured environmentally.  
Danish municipalities are still in the process of finding the right method for collecting and recycling plastic 
waste. Studies in recycling of plastic, addressing strategies to reach the European targets, are few. BIO 
Intelligence Service (Mudgal et al., 2013) has made a roadmap for increased mechanical recycling targets 
on a European scale. In order to reach the targets set by the European Commision they advice on six 
different initiatives whereof three are reachable within the jurisdiction of the municipalities. The three 
initiaves are; “Collect all plastic waste separately; Efficiently sort all collected plastic waste in order to 
create appropriate quality for recycling; and Send all sorted plastic waste to certified plastics recyclers.” 
(Mudgal et al., 2013, p. 8). In agreement with this, a pre-study for this master thesis concluded that 
increasing plastic recycling could be handled at different levels of the life cycle. One of the recommended 
initiatives for Denmark was to focus on collection of high quality plastics, as great potentials for 
improvements lie there (Jakobsen, 2014). The European targets are defined in quantity, not quality, even 
though one of the major concerns in circular economy is sustainable recycling, which cannot be measured 
==PAGE:17==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 3 of 110 
in amounts of recycling. The quantitative goals need to be supplied by quality targets. The municipalities 
are missing a guideline to decide upon both tactical short-term and strategic long-term decisions 
regarding plastic waste management. A hierarchy of plastic based on quality is missing to help in this 
process. 
1.1. 
Objectives 
The general objective of this thesis is to investigate how it is technically feasible to recycle household 
plastic waste in Denmark into high quality secondary raw material. This objective is aligned with the long-
term goal of closing the plastic recycling loop, following political thoughts in circular economy. The 
following seven specific objectives were established to guide the accomplishment of the thesis’ main 
objective: 
1. The first objective is to define quality in mechanical recycling of household plastic waste.  
2. Secondly, this thesis wishes to investigate the quantity targets suggested by the European Commission 
in the Circular Economy Package. What are the potential pitfalls of setting quantity targets?  
3. The third specific objective is to map the technical limitations to quality in the upgrading process. 
Furthermore, the knowledge gaps will be recorded. 
4. Fourthly, solutions to the technical challenges, both within and out of reach of the municipalities, are 
to be assessed.  Looking at the plastic system in the reverse order of process, will answer how to 
improve quality of the recycled plastic. 
5. Fifthly, developing a methodology to quantify quality of plastic is necessary to measure current level 
of high quality recycling, measure development and estimate the potential of improvement. 
Additionally making a quality hierarchy to compare grades of plastic is desired. 
6. Sixtly, the implications of increased quality on the environmental performance of mechanical 
recycling will be discussed. 
7. Lastly, this thesis aim at formulating recommendations for the municipalities by using the knowledge 
gained on quality. Others parameters, especially environmental considerations, will be put into 
perspective.  
1.2. 
Quality definition 
There are many definitions of quality in recycling flourishing in literature. They focus in different 
characteristics, like in the examples here given. Ravi (2012, p. 146) focuses on the economic value of the 
recycled plastic: “Recycling quality is that which emphasizes upon maximum retention of value from 
recyclates for producing high quality recycled products with relatively low impact in the environment”. 
Differently Mudgal and colleagues (2013, p. 17) consider impurities affecting properties: “Quality is a 
subjective term, which varies among recyclers depending on the equipment and processes used and the 
markets sold into. It can be understood as the level of impurities in the waste stream, which can affect 
mechanical and visual properties”. The quality definition by the American Society for Quality Control is: 
“Quality is the totality of features and characteristics of a product or service that bear on its ability to 
satisfy stated or implied needs” (Appelqvist, 2014), it implies that the market demand is of importance to 
quality.  
It is seen with the previous quotes that quality in recycling can be defined very differently in respect to 
the parameters evaluating quality. The cost of recycled plastic follows the virgin plastic; hence this way 
of quantifying quality would depend on external parameters like oil prices as well. Economic assessment is 
therefore not reliable, but can give a quick estimation of the quality. The impurities and their effects on 
the properties makes a better approximation of the quality, though the definition by Mudgal et al. (2013) 
does not include all relevant properties, which are described in Chapter 2. The market demands could be 
==PAGE:18==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 4 of 110 
defined as requirements to plastic properties, but these are not well defined currently. Furthermore, 
these parameters are not persistent meaning the definition of quality would also be time dependent.  
The definition is very important in terms of which actions are put into motion in order to improve quality. 
Several of the European and national legislation and strategies touch upon quality in recycling, but none 
of them defines what high quality recycling is. This means the politicians have a goal which is not defined, 
hence realising the goal will be difficult as the necessary changes are unclear. Defining what is actually 
needed would make the definition of good quality. The Waste Framework Directive states “Member States 
shall take measures to promote high quality recycling and, to this end, shall set up separate collections 
of waste where technically, environmentally and economically practicable and appropriate to meet the 
necessary quality standards for the relevant recycling sectors.” (European Commission, 2008, p. L 
312/13). The now discarded proposal from 2014 for the amendment of the directives on waste, landfill of 
waste, packaging and packaging waste, batteries and WEEE says that “..development of a comprehensive 
strategy [..] ensuring high quality recycling and the use of recycled waste as a major, reliable source of 
raw material for the Union” is necessary (European Commission, 2014a, p. 0).  Furthermore, the green 
paper on plastic waste have a suggestion for the improvement of quality; “An adequate flow of 
information from producers to recyclers is also important. Clear safety data sheets for plastic pellets 
used by converters could enhance high quality recycling.” (European Commission, 2013, p. 13). The 
Danish Resource strategy has quality in recycling as a major focus area; a milestone for 2020 is to have 
achieved high quality and an initiative to find good examples of recycling which ensure new products of 
high quality (Justesen and Nielsen, 2014). Again, here there is no definition of the term. 
The limitations, found in Chapter 6 in this thesis, determine that quality depend on the incoming 
material, which in turn means all steps in the value chain should be considered in order to achieve a high 
quality. Based on this and the findings in the following thesis, the definition of “high quality mechanical 
recycling” in this current report is: 
High quality mechanical plastic recycling should prepare materials which can 
substitute virgin materials within similar applications. In other words, the 
recycled plastics need to meet the customer’s expectations concerning 
physical, mechanical, chemical, thermal, weathering and optical properties. In 
ensuring this, a closed loop system can be achieved. This in turn depends on 
the processes performed at the sorting and recycling facilities as well as the 
segregation and the collection scheme. Furthermore, it depends upon the 
initial production and manufacture of the product that later become waste. 
The demand of raw material varies over time, hence as quality depends upon 
this, the definition of quality is not constant.  Replacing virgin materials at a 
high substitution rate is also important to ensure a high environmental 
performance. 
 
The definition is in line with the long term vision of the circular economy framework to have a closed 
material loop. 
1.3. 
Other main definitions 
Here main definitions are presented. Else the thesis refers to definitions in “ISO 472 Plastics – Vocabulary” 
(Dansk standard, 2013). 
==PAGE:19==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 5 of 110 
1.3.1. Plastic and polymers 
Polymers are the chemical definition of a chain of repeating molecular units of monomers. Differently 
plastic is the material that consist of polymers as well as added substances which is bound to the polymer 
matrix, such as additives like plasticisers, fillers, colorants and barrier agents. Plastic types is hence 
plastic of different polymers. 
1.3.2. Upgrading process 
The upgrading process is the part of the mechanical recycling, where polymers’ properties are 
intentionally improved. This could happen via means of re-stabilisation (adding stabilisers), impact 
modification (improving mechanical properties), adding fillers, compatibilisation or reactive repair 
modules. This process most often take place in the extruder. This could happen at the recycling facility 
and/or at the manufacturer. Further details are found in Chapter 2.2.2. 
1.3.3. Household waste 
Household waste is a waste type within the waste category “residential waste”, which also covers garden, 
bulky and hazardous waste, i.e. these waste types are excluded from the scope.  
1.3.4. Household plastic waste 
Household plastic waste is the material fraction within the waste type “household waste”. This excludes 
plastics found in other material fractions, e.g. Waste of Electrical and Electronic Equipment [WEEE]. 
1.3.5. Packaging 
Packaging is in the directive for packaging and packaging waste defined as: “all products made of any 
materials of any nature to be used for the containment, protection, handling, delivery and presentation 
of goods, from raw materials to processed goods, from the producer to the user or the consumer.” 
(European Parliament and Council, 1994, p. L 365/13).  
1.3.6. Substitution 
Substitution signify a replacement; a substitute.  In recycling methodology substitution is oftentimes 
divided into two (Damgaard, 2013; Grosso and Consonni, 2014): 
 
Technical substitution: the amount of the incoming primary material which is sorted in the sorting 
process. This term covers the material losses during recycling, i.e. quantity. 
 
Market substitution: is on the other hand how much of a virgin material the recycled material can 
replace, i.e. quality. The quantification is based on one of the following considerations; 
o 
Economical: the difference in economic value from the virgin to the recycled material. 
o 
Technical: the properties of the recycled material compared to the virgin material. 
1.4. 
Scope 
The scope will be limited to the household plastic covering both plastic in residual and source-segregated 
waste from households. The geographical scope is European, but based on Danish conditions and data. The 
focus will be on mechanical recycling, as this is the most common recycling method with around 3,000 
companies in Europe undertaking mechanical recycling activities (Ignatyev et al., 2014). Within recycling 
the upgrading process will be investigated in detail, as this is where improvement of the quality is 
assumed to take place. Currently the focus from municipalities is on sorting efficiencies, but there is no 
focus on the further processes. This could be caused by the focus on quantity. There are multiple barriers 
to recycling, but the focus of this report will be the technical limitations and possibilities. 
==PAGE:20==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 6 of 110 
There are numerous types of plastic, hence the report focus on the five primary polymers consumed in  
households (Jakobsen, 2011; PlasticsEurope, 2013), excluding polyvinyl chloride [PVC]. The five polymers 
in focus are: 
 
Polyethylene terephthalate [PET] 
 
High density polyethylene [HDPE] 
 
Low density polyethylene [LDPE] 
 
Polypropylene [PP] 
 
Polystyrene [PS] 
The polymers can be based on both fossil and renewable sources. The so-called biobased polymers are 
included in the scope, as they are chemically similar to the polymers from fossil sources. Biobased plastic 
are though often not made as one of the five polymers, but are commonly found as e.g. polylactic acid 
[PLA] or poly-3-hydroxybuturate [PHB], and are therefore not a part of the scope. However, polyethylene 
derived from bioethanol would be in the scope (Christensen and Fruergaard, 2010; European Commission, 
2013; European PET Bottle Platform, 2014). 
1.4.1. Out of scope 
The following elements are out of the scope of this report: 
1.4.1.1. Feedstock recycling 
Feedstock recycling is eliminated from the scope because it covers a small fraction of the household 
plastic recycling, and that there is no indication Danish plastic waste currently undergo this form of 
recycling (Jakobsen, 2014). Feedstock recycling, also known as chemical recycling, is by Mudgal and 
colleagues (2011, p. 3) defined as “...techniques used to break down plastic polymers into their 
constituent monomers, which in turn can be used again in refineries, or petrochemical and chemical 
production.”. There are different types of chemical processes, examples are pyrolysis, hydrolysis, 
hydrocracking and gasification (Al-Salem et al., 2010; Brems et al., 2012). In Europe chemical or feedstock 
recycling only recycles 0.5% and 0.3% of packaging and post-consumer plastic waste respectively. This 
form of recycling is increasing at a fast rate, 19.4% from 2011 to 2012 (PlasticsEurope, 2013), but it still a 
small share of the recycling. Geographically feedstock recycling of packaging is limited to Germany in 
Europe, but is more widespread elsewhere in the world, e.g. in Japan where 5% of the waste plastic is 
chemically recycled (Joint Research Centre - European Commission, 2013a). Furthermore polymer-wise, 
feedstock seems limited to mainly nylon (PA6), e.g. the German company (Polyamid 2000 AG in Permnitz) 
chemically recycles PA6 from carpets. 
1.4.1.2. Polyvinyl chloride  
Polyvinyl chloride [PVC] is excluded from this study because it normally is recycled at specialised facilities 
due to the complex process and is collected separately outside the common source segregated collection. 
Of the 16 PVC facilities certified by EuCert all exclusively handled PVC (EuCertPlast, 2014). PVC is one of 
the major polymers, but is not easily mechanically recyclable at ordinary recycling plants. There are a 
number of barriers to recycling, e.g. that PVC release HCl during degradation and furthermore, a wide 
range of additives hinders separation in recycling (La Mantia, 2002). 
1.4.1.3. Thermoset plastics  
Thermoset plastic is not mechanically recyclable and will not be included further in this report. Plastics 
are divided into two main groups - thermoplastic and thermoset plastic – according to their thermal 
processing behaviour. In general, thermoplastics constitute 90% of the consumption in Europe, while 
thermoset plastics are the remaining 10% (Kaysen & Tønning 2011). The difference between them is the 
chemical structure, where thermoplastic is composed of long flexible polymer chains, in thermoset plastic 
==PAGE:21==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 7 of 110 
the polymers is positioned in a cross linked structure. The chemical difference also means thermoplastics 
can change structure when temperature and pressure is increased, whereas thermoset plastic is more 
stable and resistant to mechanical force, heat, chemicals and general wear. Thermoset plastic more often 
contain fillers, like glass fibre, to compensate for the low toughness and brittleness (Christensen and 
Fruergaard, 2010; Harper, 1996; Joint Research Centre - European Commission, 2013b).  
==PAGE:22==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 8 of 110 
2. Theory 
In this chapter three main parts will be covered; 1) basic plastic theory, 2) mechanical recycling with 
focus on upgrading and 3) legislation regarding mechanical plastic recycling in Denmark and in the EU. 
2.1. 
Plastic 
Reviewing a value chain of plastic products the plastic is first produced, and then manufactured into a 
product, thereafter becomes waste and in the end goes into waste treatment. In this subchapter a short 
description of production and conversion is given, a short introduction to consumption of plastic, then a 
description of plastic properties, which are important when looking at mechanical recycling and lastly, a 
characterisation of plastic waste from households. 
2.1.1. Production and conversion 
The primary raw material to produce polymers is fossil oil; however, gas is used to a lesser extent. Only 
0.1-0.2% of all plastic is currently derived from renewable organic sources, such as starch (Joint Research 
Centre - European Commission, 2013b). The oil or gas is converted in a refinery to, among other products, 
naphtha. Naphtha is a group of liquid hydrocarbons that includes the lightest and most volatile fractions in 
petroleum. Hereafter the naphtha is cracked into monomers. The monomers, e.g. ethylene and propylene, 
are then converted into polymers, i.e. chains of repeating molecular units of monomers. This can happen 
via different techniques, e.g. in a chemically inert solution or via suspension where a catalyst is initiated 
in water. Polymers contain, apart from hydrogen and carbon, often oxygen, nitrogen, chlorine or sulphur. 
In addition to polymer, additives are often added to the polymers in order to enhance properties, improve 
processing or add to visual appearance. There is a vast list of additives and the amount and types in a 
specific plastic depend on the application and plastic type (Harper, 1996; Joint Research Centre - 
European Commission, 2013b; Merrild et al., 2012). This will be investigated further in the chapter 
regarding limitations to quality in upgrading (Chapter 6). There is only a few major plants producing 
plastic in the world (Christensen and Fruergaard, 2010), hence the great confidentiality regarding the 
actual composition of plastic to retain their market share and due to complex production. More industries 
perform the operation of converting plastic into e.g. granulates, as the process is simpler compared to the 
production of plastic. Plastic items are afterwards manufactured with different conversion techniques, 
e.g. injection moulding, casting, blow moulding, film blowing and extrusion. The chosen technique 
depends on the plastic item desirable (Christensen and Fruergaard, 2010; Joint Research Centre - 
European Commission, 2013b). The plastic products are in use for a shorter or longer time period 
depending on the application before they become obsolete and are considered waste. 
2.1.2. Consumption of plastic in households 
There is an estimated 15,000 plastic types, which are used to a higher or lower extent (Shah, 1998). The 
six most common plastic types in post-consumer waste, which also includes waste from the commercial 
sector, is PET, HDPE, PVC, PP and PS (PlasticsEurope, 2013), meaning there are a vast number of the 
existing plastic types, which are irrelevant for this study. These six polymers have an identification 
system, which was introduced by the American Society of the Plastics Industry in 1988, which has since 
spread. The plastic is classified by chemical polymer structure, presented in Table 2.1. The labelling is 
mandatory in a number of countries for packaging, but in the European Union marking is voluntary 
(Christensen and Fruergaard, 2010). Furthermore, common applications of virgin and recycled plastics are 
displayed in Table 2.1. End use of the recycled plastic is dependent upon the quality of plastic, the 
mixture of polymers and the degree of contamination (La Mantia, 2002). PVC, with symbol no. 3, has been 
excluded because the polymer is out of scope of this report, as described in the introduction.

the consumption covers both the households and the commercial sector, and furthermore, that the data 
on household packaging is from one municipality only. It is unknown if the composition of plastic 
packaging should vary from region to region in Denmark.

2.1.2.2. Non-packaging plastic 
The non-packaging plastics consist of different commodities, and the data is limited. The Joint Research 
Centre of the European Commission (2013a) indicates non-packaging plastic waste consists of houseware 
items and misplaced WEEE and PlasticsEurope (2013) mentions household appliances, furniture and sport 
items. Additionally, MEPEX (2013) indicates furniture, sport and leisure while Vestforbrænding postulates 
this could range from bowls, toys, hangers, toothbrushes to furniture (Schøt-Nielsen, 2014). In Figure 2.2 
the plastic types in non-packaging are indicated by PlasticsEurope (2013) in “Others” segment, covering 
all other plastic demand except from packaging, building & construction, automobile and electrical & 
electronic. There is agreement that the largest fraction of identified polymers is PP when comparing to 
findings by Edjabou et al. (2014). Furthermore, Figure 2.2 indicated PUR and LDPE to be large fractions. 
There is great uncertainty regarding these figures, as products in “Others” segment would not only, when 
obsolete, end as household waste, but in other waste categories as well. Additionally most of the 
polymers in the non-packaging plastic waste, in Figure 2.2, are not determined.

2.1.3. Plastic properties  
Great strength and flexibility are crucial features of plastic (Christensen and Fruergaard, 2010), but 
plastic have many other properties. Whole books, like “Handbook of plastics testing technology” (Shah, 
1998), describe different properties and therefore, not all properties can be described here. Firstly, the 
morphology of polymers will be described, as the other properties are oftentimes dependent upon the 
morphology. Later other properties relevant for plastics found in household plastic waste are described 
and lastly the difference in properties depending on the polymer. Knowledge regarding the plastic 
properties is important for recycling into high quality products as upgrading the loss in properties results 
in higher qualities and hence a wider application range. 
2.1.3.1. Morphology 
Plastic are often classified by their chemical polymer structure; the basic chemical structure shown earlier 
in Table 2.1 is not enough to characterize a polymer; PET is not just PET, but can e.g. be amorphous or 
crystalline and in combination with different amounts of additives. Morphology of a polymer depends 
mainly on chemical composition, degree of polymerization and chain conformation (La Mantia, 2002). 
There is batch to batch variations in virgin materials as well, due to the complex polymerization process 
(Daugaard, 2014).  
 
FIGURE 2.3. SKETCHES OF AMORPHOUS, SEMI-CRYSTALLINE AND CRYSTALLINE POLYMER.

Depending on the production method of polymers it is possible to achieve amorphous, semi-crystalline and 
crystalline polymers as shown in Figure 2.3. The ability of a polymer to crystalize is mainly determined by 
regular placement of atoms in the chain. Polymers made of symmetrical unsaturated monomers crystallize 
easily, e.g. PE, which is seen in Table 2.1 being the only polymer with no substituents. On the other hand 
asymmetrical polymers, like PP, crystalize only within regular configuration (isotactic or syndiotactic), 
while atactic (random placed substituents) PP is amorphous, see Figure 2.4. In reality these are 
theoretical constellations, as a certain amount of atactic material will always be present (Dansk standard, 
2013; Harper, 1996). 
 
FIGURE 2.4. ISOTACTIC WITH REGULAR PLACED SUBSTITUENTS AND SYNDIOTACTIC WITH CHANGING PLACE OF 
SUBSTITUENTS (WIKIPEDIA, 2007A, 2007B). 
Amorphous polymers are homogenous, and are at low temperature glassy, hard and brittle, but with 
increasing temperature they get rubbery, soft and elastic. There is no melting point, but they have a 
smooth transition. Examples of amorphous polymers are atactic PS and A-PET (Harper, 1996). 
Semicrystalline are amorphous polymers with crystalline regions. Examples of semicrystalline polymers are 
LDPE, HDPE and C-PET (Harper, 1996). Semicrystalline or crystalline polymers are heterogeneous and 
occur more frequently in packaging. 
Many properties of polymers depend on the morphology; when crystallinity increases the following also 
increase: density, permeability, opacity, strength, heat sealing temperature; whereas the following 
decrease: blocking, clarity, tear strength, impact strength, toughness, ductility, ultimate elongation and 
heat sealing temperature ranges. Polymers have a isotropic behaviour, which means the mechanical, 
barrier and optical properties are different along different axes. The reason being the strong covalent 
bonds in the backbone chain and weak intermolecular forces. Testing the properties of plastic is therefore 
not straightforward. 
2.1.3.2. Other properties  
The main properties of plastic, evaluated to be most important for household plastic waste, has been 
divided into six property categories. In order to evaluate the quality of plastics it is important to look the 
following three items: properties, the loss in properties during use and how properties can be upgraded 
during the recycling process to previous state. The polymers basic properties are described in this section. 
The polymers are often improved with additives, which will be discussed later in Chapter 6 regarding 
limitations to recycling. It is possible to manipulate all properties with additives. 
 
Physical properties, 
 
Mechanical properties, 
 
Chemical properties, 
 
Thermal properties, 
 
Weathering properties and 
 
Optical properties. 
 
==PAGE:28==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 14 of 110 
Physical properties regard mainly the density (weight per unit volume (Shah, 1998)), water absorption 
potential and shape (Harper, 1996). 
Mechanical properties are important as most products involve mechanical load. Mechanical properties can 
best be explained as the relation between strain and stress as seen in Figure 2.5 below. Strain is change in 
length per unit of original length, where stress is the force applied in a unit area (Shah, 1998). 
 
FIGURE 2.5. STRESS STRAIN GRAPH. AFTER (HARPER, 1996, FIG. 
8.8). 
Plastic material classifies after softness, brittleness, hardness and toughness, as seen in Table 2.3 below. 
Often plastics are distinguished between flexible and rigid, but the distinction is rather qualitative as 
there is no technical limit distinguishing the two. It is be noted that flexible plastics could be recycled 
into rigid plastic. It is clear from Table 2.3 that plastics cannot be determined as having good or high 
mechanical properties, as within the mechanical properties a plastic could have both low and high specific 
mechanical properties. Each specific property is explained by Figure 2.5.

Examples of mechanical properties are;  
 
Elongation at break: increase in length produced by a tensile load (Shah, 1998), 
 
Impact strength: related to the toughness and the ability to absorb applied energy - energy 
required to fracture the polymer (Shah, 1998) and 
 
Tensile strength: “ultimate strength of a material subjected to tensile loading” (Shah, 1998, p. 
425). 
Chemical properties is the resistance to chemicals, e.g. PP has poor mechanical properties but is 
imperious to most chemicals. The resistance depends on basic polymer structure, type of polymer bond, 
degree of crystallinity, branching, distance between bonds, energy required to break bonds and effect of 
plasticizers, fillers, stabilizers and colorants (Shah, 1998). 
Compared to other common materials plastic is sensitive to changes in temperature. Thermal properties 
depend especially upon if the polymer is crystalline or amorphous, which means respectively that a 
polymer has a sharp melting point or a gradual softening range. The thermal properties therefore affect 
the processing ability greatly. Molecular orientation, molecular weight, intermolecular bonding, cross 
linking and co-polymerization also affect thermal properties of a polymer (Shah, 1998). Examples of 
thermal properties are: 
 
Thermal expansion coefficient (melt):  fractional change in length or volume per unit change in 
temperature. Fillers lower the coefficient (Shah, 1998), 
 
Glass transition temperature: approximate midpoint of the temperature range over which the 
glass transition takes place. Glass transition is the reversible change in an amorphous polymer or 
amorphous regions of a semi-crystalline polymer, from/ to a viscous or rubbery condition to/from 
a hard and relatively brittle one (Dansk standard, 2013) and 
 
Melting point: ”temperature at which crystallinity in a semi-crystalline polymer disappears on 
heating” (Dansk standard, 2013, p. 79). 
Weathering means degradation of the polymer, which can happen either during processing or during the 
service life. Environmental factors which seriously affect plastic is solar radiations (UV, IR and X-ray), 
microorganisms, bacteria, fungus, mould, high humidity, ozone, oxygen, water, thermal energy and 
pollution (Shah, 1998). 
Optical properties are e.g. if the polymer is transparent or translucent and depend on basic polymer 
structure. Generally, amorphous polymer is transparent, whereas crystalline polymer have an increased 
density; the speed of light through the polymer is decreased and therefore often translucent. Optical 
property examples are: 
 
Refractive index (Na light): ratio of the velocity of light in vacuum to its velocity in a transparent 
medium (Shah, 1998) and 
 
Colour: can be tested by the L*a*b colour system (L=brightness contrast between black and white 
[0;100], a=chromaticity between green and red [-60;60], b=chromaticity between blue and yellow 
[-60;60]) (Nakatani and Hirao, 2011). 
2.1.3.3. Difference in polymers 
There is a difference in the properties of the individual polymers, which will be described here. This is 
due to the chemical differences in morphology and the different additives. The differences explain why 
many different plastic types are used for packaging, which may have different applications and 
requirements. Some of the properties have been compared in Table 2.4, where the highest values have 
been given three plusses and the lowest one. The individual polymers cannot be determined to perform 
well in a property category, as the properties within in the category are sometimes interdependent (e.g. 
==PAGE:30==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 16 of 110 
PET have a high tensile strength, but a low impact strength) and because each polymer can be chemically 
different changing the properties (e.g. EPS is very different in properties compared to PS).  
TABLE 2.4. DIFFERENCE IN PROPERTIES OF COMMON VIRGIN POLYMERS: +++ HIGH VALUE, ++ MEDIUM VALUE AND + 
LOW VALUE (HARPER, 1996; LA MANTIA, 2002).

PET is a linear polymer, with regularity in the polymer chain structure. This increases the ability to 
crystallise (determining mechanical properties). PET has good toughness, clarity and good barrier 
properties. PET is found both in amorphous [A-PET] or crystalline [C-PET] state (Harper, 1996; La Mantia, 
2002). 
The properties of PE depend on degree of chain branching. LDPE and HDPE are the main grades of PE. 
LDPE has short and long chain branching, which limits crystallinity affecting melting range to be relatively 
broad. The chain branching means LDPE has high clarity, flexibility and ease of processing. HDPE is on the 
other hand more linear and has long chains. It has a higher degree of crystallinity, higher moisture and 
oxygen barrier, better chemical resistance and more opacity. The different grades of PE means there is a 
range of materials with considerable different properties due to difference in degree of branching, 
molecular weight and molecular weight distribution. All PE have good barrier to water, but high 
permeability for oxygen, carbon dioxide and organic vapours. The melting temperature is relatively low 
(Harper, 1996; La Mantia, 2002). 
Compared to PE, PP has lower impact strength and is sensitive to oxidation (presence of more labile H 
atoms), but superior heat resistance and tensile strength. There are three main structures of PP; isotactic, 
syndiotactic and atactic. The most common is isotactic PP [iso-PP], with the placement of all methyl 
groups on the same side of the polymer backbone. This entails highly crystalline structure, which gives 
opaque and good solvent resistance. PP is often found as a copolymer (i.e. derived from more than one 
monomer) with e.g. ethylene which increase the impact properties (Harper, 1996; La Mantia, 2002). 
The polymerization condition of PS affects purity, styrene content, molecular weight and degree of 
branching. PS is found in three main grades; solid, expanded [EPS] and high impact [HIPS]. PS is 
amorphous in nature, but the syndiotactic structure is crystalline (La Mantia, 2002). 
==PAGE:31==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 17 of 110 
2.1.4. Household plastic waste characterisation 
A waste characterisation can have different purposes, for example provide quantities and composition of 
waste to plan waste management systems or determine efficiency of a segregation scheme (Lagerkvist et 
al., 2010). In the context of this report, a characterisation is not conducted, but data is collected from 
different waste characterisations. The purpose of this section is to estimate the quantity and characterise 
the composition of the material fraction “plastic” within household waste in Denmark. This 
characterisation will give background information regarding current recycling and total waste generation 
in household plastic waste in order to make recommendations for municipalities and determine the 
recyclable fraction of the plastic waste generation. Additionally, a characterisation should touch upon 
spatial variation, temporal variation as well as uncertainties according to (Lagerkvist et al., 2010). 
2.1.4.1. Waste plastic generation rate 
In order to calculate a technical and environmental recycling potential, it is interesting to investigate the 
generation rate. The potential will later be compared to the proposed targets of recycling, set by the EU. 
The waste generation rate refers to the amount of waste generated in households. As household packaging 
is often short-lived (Joint Research Centre - European Commission, 2013b), the consumption of packaging 
gives a good indication of the waste generation rate of packaging. This is in contrast to the non-packaging 
plastics, which often have a longer service life. The difference between plastic consumption and waste 
plastic generation rate is the stock building in society. 
The plastic waste generation rates have been estimated by several authors, as can be seen in Table 2.5. 
They are based on different calculation methods and refer to different years. Some agreement in the data 
is observed, but the uncertainties are large. Furthermore, some of the data is relatively old but at least 
packaging have been stable in quantity during the last decade (Regeringen, 2015). In order to compare 
these different indications of plastic waste generation rate, calculations where performed, that are 
presented in Appendix A.

Møller and colleagues (2013) provide a plastic generation rate including non-packaging plastic in Denmark. 
Packaging amounts to 87% and is based on packaging supply statistics from 2005 (Emballageforsyningen 
2005). Supply is defined as the production in Denmark the current year as well as the import minus the 
export, and therefore gives a good indication of the consumption. Non-packaging plastic data comes from 
an analysis of residual waste (Petersen and Domela, 2003), but was in quantity increased with 17%. The 
adjustment of 17%, which was necessary in order to match the data to the registered collected waste 
==PAGE:32==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 18 of 110 
(Møller et al., 2013). The reason might be spatial or temporal uncertainties of the sample analysis, but it 
is not clarified. It was not clear which sub-fractions were included in non-packaging plastic.  
Econet calculated a potential for plastic packaging in Danish households in 2009 based on Petersen et al. 
(2014). The amount of packaging sent on the market in 2005 (imports and exports accounted for), which 
can be found in the Danish statistic of packaging supply in 2005 (Emballageforsyningen 2005), was reduced 
by 33%. The latter due the fact that the Danish EPA in a later report adjusted the amount of plastic 
packaging in 2005 to half of the original amount (Møller et al., 2013) and a consumption reduction in 
Danish households from 2005 to 2009 (Petersen et al., 2014). The consumption have risen again since 2009 
(Regeringen, 2015). 
The newest available packaging supply statistics from 2009 (Jakobsen, 2011) only have data on all 
packaging, i.e. not only from households. If the same ratio (45%) (Petersen et al., 2014) of household’s 
packaging of the total packaging is transferred to 2009, the amount of plastic packaging in households is 
as seen in Table 2.5. This is higher than estimated earlier by (Petersen et al., 2014). 
In the MFA in the pre-study, the total plastic waste consumed in households was calculated based on 
estimations, why this number have high uncertainty. The stock building amounting to 12.87 kg/hh/yr was 
excluded in the figure above (Jakobsen, 2014). 
The newest data from Edjabou et al. (2014) is a waste characterisation of residual waste from 1,442 
households of both single- and multi-family housing types from three municipalities; Aabenraa, Haderslev 
and Sønderborg. The characterisation showed insignificant differences between the municipalities. The 
source-segregated rigid plastic packaging is the data on the collected amount in 2011 in Aabenraa 
(Aabenraa Kommune, 2013). The collection scheme is the same in all three municipalities. 
The ratio of packaging and non-packaging plastic waste varies greatly in the data. According to the 
European Commission’s Institute for Prospective Technological Studies (JRC-IPTS) a significant share of 
European municipal solid waste [MSW] consists of packaging, 70% (Joint Research Centre - European 
Commission, 2013a) and the same is true for household plastic waste according to PlasticsEurope in 
(Michaud et al. 2010). According to data in Møller et al. (2013) the ratio of packaging is 87% and even 
higher, 97%, found in Edjabou et al. (2014). The packaging ratio seems very high, and this might indicate 
that data on non-packaging plastic waste is limited. The definition of packaging might also vary from 
report to report. Plastic ends in other waste fractions as they are mixed with other materials, e.g. milk 
cartons, and non-packaging plastic have a longer lifetime compared to packaging. 
Denmark has an effective refund system, Dansk Retursystem, on fizzy beverages and mineral water. A 
fraction of the plastic consumption hence does not end in the municipal waste management system. PET 
bottles are collected for high quality recycling and remanufactured into new bottles and food packaging. 
In 2013, 13,000 tonnes were collected through the system corresponding to 5 kg/hh/yr (Svenningsen, 
2014) – see Appendix B for calculation. 
2.1.4.2. Spatial variation  
An example of spatial variation is given by Econet that estimates plastic packaging consumption is 20% 
higher in the City of Frederiksberg compared to the rest of Denmark as a whole (Petersen et al., 2014) 
based on information from Statistics Denmark. Edjabou and colleagues (2014) found no significant 
differences in quantity and composition between Aabenraa, Sønderborg and Haderslev, three similar sized 
cities in Southern Jutland. Another example is that there is more plastic packaging in single family 
households compared to multi-family households, but similar amounts of non-packaging plastic according 
to a waste characterisation by the Danish EPA in 2003 (Møller et al., 2013). Edjabou and colleagues (2014) 
agrees to the fact that there is more plastic packaging in single-family households, which was especially 
==PAGE:33==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 19 of 110 
explained by higher amounts of plastic films. Contradictory the waste characterisation conducted showed 
higher amounts of non-packaging plastic in multi-family households matched to single-family households 
(Edjabou et al., 2014). One reason for the difference between single and multi-family houses could be the 
difference in affluence, but other potential explanations is also valid. That the consumption is higher in 
Frederiksberg contradict the fact that more packaging is generated in single-family houses, as 
Frederiksberg mainly consists of multi-family buildings. Other factors like affluence and culture (e.g. the 
culture of “on-the-go” and take-away food and beverages, creating large quantities of packaging, is more 
common in city areas) might play a role as well. This indicates there could be spatial differences of 
quantity between the cities of different regions in Denmark, and between the residence types. 
2.1.4.3. Temporal variation 
There has been a continuous growth of plastic consumption through the last 50 years, though the 
production has been affected by the crisis recently (PlasticsEurope, 2013). In Europe distribution of 
demand of polymers for packaging has been unchanged the last decade (Joint Research Centre - European 
Commission, 2013b). This must be assumed similar to Denmark. The indications of commodities in non-
packaging plastics results in the conclusion that non-packaging plastic is items with longer lifetime and is 
further supported by studies (Mutha et al., 2006; Patel et al., 1998). Hence supply cannot be an accurate 
measure of non-packaging waste generation rate. In the pre-study MFA, it was calculated that there is a 
stock generation currently, which likely consist of mainly non-packaging plastic products, as packaging is 
estimated to have an average lifetime of 2 years (Jakobsen, 2014). In the future, part of this in-use stock 
will become obsolete and non-packaging plastic waste is assumed to increase. 
The Council of Europe has observed seasonal changes of the amount of waste plastic in MSW in Europe. In 
Central Europe MSW consisted of 9.6% plastic in winter, and increased to 10.5% in summer. The same 
trend was observed in Eastern Europe, where plastic waste accounted to 5.0% in winter, but 13.2% in 
summer (nations included in Central and Eastern Europe not indicated) (Joint Research Centre - European 
Commission, 2013a). The reason could be an increase in beverage consumption in summer, if this is the 
main explanation, then it is expected that the variation are smaller in Denmark, as most beverage 
containers are handled through Dansk Retursystem instead municipal waste systems.  
2.1.4.4. Uncertainty 
In general, great uncertainty is seen regarding the characterisation of household plastics. There are data 
lacks of non-packaging plastics; concerning both the generation rate and the composition. Data on plastic 
packaging is oftentimes not separated into commercial and household waste, hence the composition is 
uncertain. 
2.2. 
Recycling 
Recycling is defined by Waste Framework Directive 2008/98/EC (European Commission, 2008, p. L 312/10) 
as follows:  
 “recovery operation by which waste materials are reprocessed into products, materials 
or substances whether for the original or other purposes. It includes the reprocessing of 
the material but does not include energy recover and the reprocessing into materials that 
are to be used as fuels or for backfilling operations.“  
The terminology of recycling is still discussed, e.g. with large perception differences in terms of recycling 
and downcycling. Further discussion on this matter will be in the Chapter 7 related to quality aspects. The 
different recycling options will here be presented as well as details on mechanical recycling and especially 
the upgrading process. 
==PAGE:34==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 20 of 110 
2.2.1. Recycling options 
Recycling can be divided into three types of plastic treatment technologies, which will be described 
briefly below (Appelqvist, 2014). In Figure 2.6 below the three recycling possibilities are sketched on the 
value chain of plastic. 
Primary aka Re-extrusion: clean scrap of a single plastic type can be introduced directly to the extrusion 
cycle. This type of recycling is commonly termed “internal recycling” and can be seen at facilities 
manufacturing plastic products (Al-Salem et al., 2009). It is questionable whether the re-extrusion should 
be regarded as recycling, since the material is not a product yet and may not be regarded as waste. 
Furthermore, low converting efficiency in a facility would result in large quantity of rejects and yield high 
recycling rates, which is not desired due to lower through put and higher costs. 
Secondary aka Mechanical: plastic waste and scrap is sorted and reprocessed by mechanical means 
(shredding, melting, granulation) into pellets, flakes, powders or granulates. The polymer structure is not 
changed due to any chemical processes (Al-Salem et al., 2009; Michaud et al., 2010; Mudgal et al., 2011; 
PlasticsEurope, 2014a). Only thermoplastics goes through mechanical recycling (Villanueva et al., 2010). 
Tertiary aka Chemical or Feedstock: the most advanced facilities chemically reduce the plastics (through 
e.g. gasification or pyrolysis) into monomers which can serve as feedstock for production of virtually any 
polymer. This means plastics which are difficult to mechanically recycle, can be chemically recycled 
(PlasticsEurope, 2014b). 
 
FIGURE 2.6. THE PLASTIC VALUE CHAIN SHOWING THE DIFFERENT RECYCLING 
POSSIBILITIES. THIS IS NOT REPRESENTATIVE FOR ALL PLASTICS, AS SOME PROCESSES 
MIGHT NOT BE INVOLVED OR THERE MIGHT BE ADDITIONAL PROCESSES. 
==PAGE:35==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 21 of 110 
2.2.2. Mechanical recycling in detail 
There are different practices in the mechanical recycling facilities, but a representative process line is 
described in Figure 2.7 below. This whole process chain might not occur at the same place and some 
processes, e.g. washing, might be repeated at another step. The most common outputs of the recycling 
process are displayed as well. It is observed flakes and agglomerates do not go through an upgrading 
process which may or may not be a part of the manufacture facility. The processes depend on the quality 
desired, economy and also the input materials being processed.  
 
FIGURE 2.7. PROCESSES IN MECHANICAL RECYCLING (AL-SALEM ET AL., 2009; FREUDENRICH, 
2014; HARPER, 1996; JOINT RESEARCH CENTRE - EUROPEAN COMMISSION, 2013B; 
LARDINOIS AND VAN DE KLUNDERT, 1996). THE ORDER OF THE PROCESSES VARIES FROM 
FACILITY TO FACILITY AND SOME PROCESSES COULD TAKE PLACE AT THE MANUFACTURER. 
==PAGE:36==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 22 of 110 
Each of the processing in sorting and reprocessing, as shown in Figure 2.7, is here described. 
The sorting process often start with non-plastic impurities removal, aka pre-sorting; contaminant like 
glass, paper and metals should be sorted from the plastic to get a clean stream and improve the quality. 
Different mechanical possibilities like eddy current to separate non-ferrous metals and over band magnets 
to sort ferrous metals are available. Sorting; the purpose of this step is to separate the different plastic 
types from each other. The plastic types can be sorted according to different properties, like density or 
spectroscopic properties. Polyolefins have overlapping properties, making separation more difficult. 
Density sorting could be float/sink or hydrocyclone. Spectroscopic separation can be with means of 
infrared light or x-ray (La Mantia, 2002). Size reduction; can overall happen by either cutting or shredding 
by impact. The purpose is to get a more homogenous material with similar size and shape, to facilitate 
further processing, and to separate composite materials. Volume is at the same time reduced (La Mantia, 
2002). Washing removes surface particles. High level of purity can be achieved with longer washing and 
higher temperature. Washing is followed by drying; mechanical or thermal (La Mantia, 2002).  
Agglomeration involves slight melting by means of heat or pressure. The purpose of agglomeration is to 
get a more uniform size and shape of the pellets. Furthermore, a higher apparent density is attained and 
better flowing properties (La Mantia, 2002). Extrusion has the main purpose of melting the plastics into 
the desired interim or end product, but also in recycling the purpose is upgrading in order to improve the 
properties of the plastic material. Mechanical properties and re-stabilisation of the polymer is the focus of 
recycling today, but the upgrading can include other property improvements. In the extrusion process 
stabilisers, additives, processing aids, curing agents, colours, lubricants, catalysts to improve processing, 
dyes and correction agents can be added. Re-establishing the properties of the plastic is necessary in case 
the original additives have reacted or decomposed (Joint Research Centre - European Commission, 2013a; 
La Mantia, 2002). There are five main steps in the upgrading process; re-stabilisation, impact 
modification, fillers addition, compatibilisation and reactive repair modules. 
Re-stabilisation is the process of adding stabilizer to avoid further degradation during reprocessing, 
ageing and weathering. Stabilisers added to the virgin material have been used to a varying degree 
depending on the oxidation sensitivity and the intensity and duration of ageing. Different additives 
are suitable for different polymer (blends) and depending on which product the recyclate is 
intended for (La Mantia, 2002; Vilaplana and Karlsson, 2008).  
Impact modification is the process of adding elastomers, in order to improve elongation at break 
and impact strength (La Mantia, 2002). 
Fillers are added to improve modulus, impact and tensile strength, but have to be balanced not to 
decrease process-ability and elongation at break. Fillers can either be organic or inorganic; 
examples are glass fibres, wood chips or talcum (Clemen, 2014; La Mantia, 2002; Vilaplana and 
Karlsson, 2008).  
Compatibilisation, adding compatibilisers to polymer blends, either non-reactive or reactive, to 
improve compatibility and create a stable structure. This is achieved by reducing the interfacial 
tension, by modifying the polymers’ interfaces. This will be described further in Chapter 6 on 
limitations to quality in upgrading. Upgrading often also happens by blending with virgin polymer, 
where compatibilisers can be necessary to add in order to get a homogenous blend (La Mantia, 
2002; Vilaplana and Karlsson, 2008). 
Reactive repair modules – upgrading degraded components with condensation catalysts, building up 
the molecules again, can only be done with poly-condensation polymers, like PET and PA. Other 
polymers can be upgraded by branching or crosslinking reactions which can be achieved by adding 
==PAGE:37==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 23 of 110 
e.g. radical generators or mixtures with reactive functional groups (La Mantia, 2002; Vilaplana and 
Karlsson, 2008).  
Melt filtration might also be a part of extrusion, which removes foreign objects and difficult to melt 
polymers. Granulation or pelletisation happens in the end of the extrusion, where either a hot- or cold-
cut is applied. Hot-cut is where the melt is still warm and opposite cold-cut where the melt is cooled 
before cutting into pellets/granulates. There is a great difference in the output of the recycling facility. 
Of what is mechanically recycled, 87% (Europe, 2002-2003) is converted into an intermediate shape (e.g. 
regrind, flakes, agglomerate, pellets, regranulates and powder) dependent on the further process, the 
manufacture technology and application. The remaining 13% is introduced directly into low value products 
like floor covering and plant pots according to Aguado and co-workers (2007). It must be assumed 
introducing waste directly into production rarely happens with post-consumer waste because of the 
heterogeneous mixture, why these 13% actually falls under primary recycling and not mechanical recycling 
referring to the definitions earlier in this subchapter. 
2.3. 
Legalisation  
Legislation affects all levels of the plastics life cycle and is often regarded as contradictory and 
insufficient. The Nordic Council of Ministers (2013) mentions subsidies for alternative waste treatment, 
insufficient requirements for recycling and illegal export of plastic waste out of Europe. The plethora of 
laws and potential contradictions between them make it complex for the individual company to keep track 
of latest developments in legislative framework related to plastics recycling. In regards to recycling of 
plastic waste from households, the following directives and acts are central.  
 
EU packaging directive (94/62/EC). Implemented in 1994. The directive “..aims to harmonize 
national measures in order to prevent or reduce the impact of packaging and packaging waste on the 
environment and to ensure the functioning of the Internal Market.  It contains provisions on the 
prevention of packaging waste, on the re-use of packaging and on the recovery and recycling of 
packaging waste.” (European Commission, 2011a). The goals for 2008 for collection of packaging 
plastic was 22.5%, which should go to recycling or incineration with energy recovery. This goal have 
not been updated (European Commission, 2011a). Recently a reduction of plastic bags have been 
proposed and an amendment is expected in 2015 (European Commission, 2015, 2014b). This goal 
seem to have been beneficial as the recycling rates for packaging waste is higher than the total 
recycling. Furthermore, there is, in Act no. 1049 of 10. November 2011 on certain demands for 
packaging, some demands to the design of packaging and limitations on heavy metals which should 
make the packaging easier to recycle (Justesen and Nielsen, 2014; Miljøministeriet, 2011). 
 
Waste Framework Directive (2008/98/EC) [WFD]. Member states should make a waste strategy 
involving life cycle thinking and a key principle of the directive is extended producer responsibility. 
The directive is in Denmark implemented through the Environmental Protection Act. 
o 
End of Waste criteria. “End-of-waste criteria specify when certain waste ceases to be 
waste and obtains a status of a product (or a secondary raw material)” (European 
Commission, 2014c). An end of waste criteria was recently released for plastic (European 
Commission, 2014c).  
 
Danish Environmental Protection Act. Hereunder the Danish Waste Act. Contains, among other, 
regulations for the municipalities to collect and recycle plastics, referring only to recyclable plastic, 
but without defining this term (Miljøministeriet, 2012). Municipalities must make waste strategies 
every 6th year with a 12 year plan (Justesen and Nielsen, 2014). 
 
Furthermore, strategies and political statements might give an idea of the acts and directives to be 
potentially released. Worth mentioning are: 
 
Green Paper on a European Strategy on plastic waste in the Environment. This European 
Commission green paper on plastic waste published in early 2013 was a starting point to review the 
existing waste legislation. It has suggestions on e.g. ban on landfill of plastic waste, but not at the 
==PAGE:38==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 24 of 110 
consequence of all plastic being incinerated. The paper also suggested further targets on plastic 
beyond packaging, which has come with the WFD. Discusses different obstacles such as missing 
information flow from producers to recyclers, biodegradable plastics, and suggestions to future work, 
e.g. investigate if chemical design changes could improve recyclability (European Commission, 2013). 
 
Proposal for a DIRECTIVE OF THE EUROPEAN PARLIAMENT AND OF THE COUNCIL amending 
Directives 2008/98/EC on waste, 94/62/EC on packaging and packaging waste, 1999/31/EC on the 
landfill of waste, 2000/53/EC on end-of-life vehicles, 2006/66/EC on batteries and accumulators 
and waste batteries and accumulators, and 2012/19/EU on waste electrical and electronic 
equipment. This proposal was presented in July 2014, was put on hold in December 2014 and latest 
news as of January 2015 is a possible, not confirmed, withdrawal (DAKOFA, 2015b).  A new proposal is 
expected in 2015. Targets for plastic packaging were set to 45% for preparing for reuse and recycling 
by 2020, and rising to 60% in 2025. Within municipal waste the proposal suggests fixing recycling or 
preparation for reuse targets at 50% for recyclable fractions stated as plastics, metals, paper and 
glass in total within 2020, and 70% in 2030 (European Commission, 2014a, 2014d). 
 
Moving towards a circular economy. The so-called “Circular Economy Package”. This European 
Commission communication described a zero waste programme for a Europe where nothing is wasted, 
natural resources is managed in a sustainable way, there is an increase in resource efficiency, the 
amount of waste is limited, there is investments in green technology, moving towards less resource 
consuming and a more climate friendly society (Justesen and Nielsen, 2014). 
 
Danish Resource strategy. When waste is recycled the material should become products of high 
quality and the materials should stay in the loop (Justesen and Nielsen, 2014) 
 
Roadmap to a Resource Efficient Europe. One of the milestones for 2020 is high quality recycling 
(European Commission, 2011b). 
2.3.1. Legislation on hazardous substances 
Plastic recyclers need to apply to the chemical legislation REACH. REACH is the abbreviation for 
Registration, Evaluation and Authorisation of CHemicals. It obligate plastic recyclers to provide safety 
data sheets, but they are exempted to register all substances (Plastics Recyclers Europe, 2015).Hazardous 
substances in recycled plastic limit the application possibilities. In Europe EFSA regulates the 
requirements for food approved plastics. A challenge test is performed at the recycling facilities. If the 
decontamination process is efficient enough to reduce 3 mg/kg PET of a surrogate to a level below the 
maximum, the recycling process can be approved to recycle food contact plastic. There is furthermore a 
requirement that the input material does not contain more than 5% non-food consumer applications (EFSA 
Panel on Food Contact Materials, Enzymes, 2011).  
 
==PAGE:39==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 25 of 110 
3. Methodology 
This chapter will introduce the methodology to the present master thesis. Theory is built upon literature 
and interviews. The methodology for the two analyses will also be described briefly here and in further 
detail later. 
3.1. 
Literature 
Literature was found via internet searches using primarily DTU Findit, which have 168 million articles, 
journals and books. Mainly newer literature was used as the mechanical recycling industry has just 
recently emerged. All information was evaluated critically in regards to the source, the methodology, and 
their references. The theoretical investigation has been limited by time. 
There is a great number of studies on plastic mechanical recycling (Astrup et al., 2009; Hopewell et al., 
2009), but the plastic sources are often unclear, i.e. industrial scrap and household plastic waste is not 
distinguished. Furthermore, quality is often discussed, but not investigated. Major research contributions 
to quality in plastic recycling has been performed by 
 
La Mantia, Francesco Paolo, Professor, University of Palermo (Palermo, Italy), 
 
Karlsson, Sigbritt, Professor, KTH Royal Institute of Technology (Stockholm, Sweden), 
 
Vilaplana, Francisco, PhD and Senior Scientist, KTH Royal Institute of Technology (Stockholm, 
Sweden) and 
 
Briassoulis, Demetres, Professor, Agricultural University of Athens (Athens, Greece). 
Additionally, there are a great number of associations and organisations related to plastic waste 
management, which are a good source of information. WRAP is an organisation with multiple publications 
on waste management, also regarding quality in plastic recycling (Dvorak et al., 2013; Foster et al., 2013; 
Freegard et al., 2014; WRAP, 2014). The EU Life+ project Plastic Zero is also a source of knowledge 
(Plastic ZERO, 2014). The European Commission has recently published an End of Waste criteria developed 
by Villanueva and Eder (2014). Industrial organisations include The Danish Plastics Federation and the 
European PET Bottle Platform.
