FIGURE 3.1. INTERVIEWS PERFORMED DISPLAYED IN THE PLASTIC VALUE CHAIN. 
The interview technique followed roughly Kvale and Brinkmann’s (2009) seven phases of interview 
investigation, but more loosely followed the last three steps:  
1. Thematisation, or the purpose 
2. Design of the interview 
3. Carrying out of interviews 
4. Transcription: excluded, but resumes were written for own use. This mean thorough analysis of 
the stakeholders’ options could not be obtained, which was neither the purpose. 
5. Analysis 
6. Verification 
7. Reporting 
The purpose of the interviews was to obtain knowledge from different stakeholders in the plastic value 
chain. This mapped the knowledge in the different steps, and what knowledge they are missing in order to 
improve quality. All interview guides have been semi-structured. This type of setup allowed an interview 
guide while still allowing room for both interviewer and interviewee to adjust the order of questions, to 
add comments outside the scope of the interview guide, and to make the necessary follow-up questions. 
==PAGE:41==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 27 of 110 
Fairly the same interview guide were used in all interviews, but difference occurred between the different 
value chain steps. Around 15 questions were asked and each interview had a length between 30 and 60 
minutes. The limitations to empirical material were time and geographical. Furthermore, no extraction, 
production, sorting facilities or reprocessing facilities which undertake household plastic are found in 
Denmark. Thus the information could be characterized as giving a one-sided picture as not all step sin the 
value chain were interviewed. Being aware of this, the report were focused on bringing in as much 
different theoretical material from literature as possible to ensure a broad overview. Some of the 
interviews were performed via phone, hence the outcome were limited during both telephone interviews 
due to lack of direct interaction. All information used in the thesis from the interviewees were verified via 
e-mail correspondence after the interview to avoid misunderstandings. When using the information 
gathered in the interviews one should be aware of the subjective nature of the answers. An interviewee 
will always speak a subjective ‘truth’. The interviews thus reflect the interviewee’s personal perception 
about the plastic recycling system. Hence different answers might have been obtained if other 
interviewees had been chosen (Kvale and Brinkmann, 2009). 
3.3. 
Scenario development 
Scenario development is used in many contexts, and it is known to be a helpful tool to describe and 
analyse future possible developments of a system. In the context of this report scenario analysis was used 
to understand the possible pathways for the Danish household plastic recycling efficiency. The scenarios 
were compared to the suggested targets on the matter that were part of the European Commission’s 
Circular Economy package. Greater details in the methodology can be found in Subchapter 5.1. 
3.4. 
Quantifying quality 
The method used to value quality from a technical perspective - in order to make a hierarchy of plastic 
types for municipalities to collect and recycle - will be briefly described here. The methodology was 
developed iteratively over the project period, since no literature regarding a method to evaluate quality 
in plastic recycling was found. 
First and foremost it is important to determine the parameters that determine quality. Quality was 
defined in the introduction, Chapter 1, by using available literature. It was found that properties of the 
plastic are important to define quality. Multiple knowledge gaps were found in regards to the losses of 
properties in the recycled material. Accordingly, quality could not be quantified by these parameters. 
Four mayor contamination groups, described in Chapter 6, affect the plastic properties: polymer cross 
contamination, additives, non-plastic impurities and degradation. Instead, quality has been evaluated on 
the contamination level of groups of commodities in the household plastic. Further explanation of the 
methodology and assumptions can be found in Section 7.2.3.1 and in Appendix E.

4. Current situation of recycling 
This chapter provides an overview of the mechanical recycling situation of household plastics today in 
Denmark. The chapter will be structured over the value chain and look at consumption, segregation at 
households, collection, sorting at recycling facilities and re-processing including upgrading, referring to 
the value chain sketched in Figure 2.6. 
The quantity and composition of plastic packaging consumption and recycling can be observed in Figure 
4.1.  The recycling rate is 29%, but in fact, this is likely rather the collection rate for plastics, as this is 
the common measurement method in Europe. It is also observed that the plastic types are recycled at 
different rates, which will be discussed later in this chapter. Denmark have a well-established recycling 
system of post-industrial plastic, while post-consumer plastic is not as commonly recycled in Denmark 
(Christensen and Fruergaard, 2010). Denmark is recycling household plastic packaging at the lowest rate in 
Europe – 3.5% in 2008 according to (Mudgal et al., 2011) and according to Plastics Europe 10.5% in 2011 
(Clemen, 2014; Lindsø, 2014). This also corresponds to findings of the MFA in the pre-study (Jakobsen, 
2014). In general plastic packaging waste has a higher recycling rate, 34.2% was mechanically recycled in 
Europe in 2012, compared to all post-consumer plastic waste, where only 26% was mechanically recycled 
(PlasticsEurope, 2013). An explanation to this trend is that plastic packaging waste is the main source of 
waste plastic because of the short lifetime (often <1 year, i.e. it becomes waste quickly) and large share 
of total consumption (Joint Research Centre - European Commission, 2013a). It cannot be confirmed if this 
is true for Denmark as well as Danish figures for post-consumer plastic waste is not available. The trend 
must be assumed similar, as household packaging plastic is a large share of the post-consumer waste and 
non-packaging plastics are evidently recycled at a lower rate than the household plastic packaging.

A minority of municipalities have the practice of collecting plastic from households; according to IKEA 
(2014) 24 of Denmark’s 98 municipalities have partly or full kerb side collection of plastics, while 
Vestforbrænding found that 27 municipalities does (Schøt-Nielsen, 2014). The remaining municipalities 
have recycling centres where the citizens can hand in plastics for recycling. Danish municipalities must 
since 2006 have a collection scheme for rigid plastics (Miljøstyrelsen, 2006), causing most municipalities to 
be in initial stages, hence data on collected plastic is limited. The collection schemes and segregation 
guidelines from the municipalities are diverse, ranging from full service collection of flexible and rigid 
packaging in Herlev, over full service collection of all rigid plastic in Frederiksberg to collection of plastic 
cans and foils at the recycling stations in Roskilde. The collection efficiencies of the total plastic waste 
generation are just as diverse, respectively 28.5% (2012-2013), 6.5% (2013) and 1.8% (2013) (Calculations 
and references can be found in Appendix B). 
Figure 4.2 below compared consumption of plastic packaging to the collection of household packaging in 
order to understand if there are differences between composition of the commercial and household 
packaging. The data is from four areas in the waste company Vestforbrænding’s area, which collect both 
rigid and flexible plastics, as well as from Aabenraa, in the waste company Arwos’s area, collecting plastic 
packaging (ARWOS, 2015). The polymer compositions of collected plastic types are shown below for all 
plastic and plastic packaging. The comparison indicates that household plastic packaging collected in 
Denmark primarily is PET, HDPE and PP. Comparing the collected plastic packaging from Aabenraa to the 
consumption it is observed that there are larger fractions of PET, HDPE and PP, and on the contrary lower 
fractions of LDPE. The unspecified plastic types could potentially be LDPE, but the fraction would still be 
smaller than the consumption. This might be explained by a difference in packaging definition. The 
missing LDPE could be also be explained by a lower collection rate than the other plastic types caused by 
difference in applications. Another explanation might be that there is a higher share of LDPE in the total 
plastic packaging compared to the household packaging, which could e.g. be caused by a large share of 
wrapping foil for transporting goods. The lower bar, on all collected plastics in Vestforbrænding’s four 
areas, indicates 24% of the collected plastic is wrap and foils, which should be primarily PE (Schøt-Nielsen, 
2014). Furthermore, a large share (23%) is mixed plastics of small wrap and foils, assumingly also LDPE. 
The sorted plastics from Vestforbrænding hence compares better to the plastic consumption.

In the City of Frederiksberg an analysis of residual waste was conducted in 2011. The rigid plastic 
packaging found in the residual waste was mainly plastics which is included in the segregation guidelines, 
but also 17-21% contaminated plastic evaluated to be non-recyclable (e.g. meat trays, bottles with danger 
pictogram and empty paint buckets). There was no indications of the plastic types, but this was reviewed 
in Aabenraa, where Figure 4.3 shows the composition of plastic types in the source-segregated and the 
residual waste. In Aabenraa, the segregation guideline includes all packaging. The residual waste 
constituted of 22% non-packaging plastic and 78% packaging. The recycling rate is 44% of the total plastic 
and 51% of the packaging (Edjabou et al., 2014). Comparing the source-segregated and the residual waste 
it was found that there are some differences indicating that PET and HDPE are sorted at higher rates and 
PP at lower rates. Again almost no LDPE was identified in the waste. The explanation about lower 
collection rates of LDPE may be eliminated, as there is no difference in LDPE shares of source-segregated 
and residual plastic waste. The other possible explanations are as before; the unidentified plastic could be 
LDPE or there is less LDPE in household packaging compared to commercial packaging. This can though be 
questioned looking at the date from Vestforbrænding in Figure 4.2.

Looking at the whole plastic recycling system, Denmark is from the author’s knowledge missing a 
household plastic sorting facility, explaining why no recyclers in Denmark accept the heterogonous 
household plastic waste. This is supported by the fact that exports was almost 14 times larger than import 
of plastic waste in 2010. Most of the export was sent to Germany (Danmarks Statistik, 2010). Commonly 
only PET, HDPE, LDPE and PP are sorted out at recycling facilities. Analysing 77 recycling facilities, 
certified by the EuCertPlast according to the European standard EN 15343 which is assumed represent 
European recycling facilities, 42% recycled LDPE, 26% HDPE, 21% PP, 19% PVC and 18% PET. Only 5% 
recycled PS, which gives an indication that the plastic type is not commonly recycled. Furthermore, the 
following plastics were recycled to a low degree: ABS, EPS, EPE and EPP (EuCertPlast, 2014). Additionally, 
the sorting facilities have great difficulties with carbon black plastic, which constitute 10% of the 
collected household rigid plastic waste in the City of Copenhagen (Bigum and Jakobsen, 2014a).

5. Scenarios of recycling efficiency 
In light of the newly proposed recycling targets from the European Commission, this chapter investigates 
possible futures of recycling of household plastic in Denmark. The scenarios are primarily based on the 
findings of the pre-study “MFA of recyclable plastic flows” that investigates limitations to reaching a high 
recycling efficiency. The pre-study concluded that Denmark have to focus on collection of high quality 
plastics and invest in an efficient sorting facility in order to increase the recycling rate. Looking at the 
value chain, from plastic waste until the recycled plastic enters new manufacture, the report in general 
indicated great losses of quantity at the collection and sorting steps of the value chain. The presented 
scenarios will therefore look at the two corresponding levels of efficiency of the collection and sorting 
step; collection and sorting efficiency. The minor losses of mass in the upgrading process are not 
considered here. 
The proposal for amendment of among others the waste directive and packaging and packaging waste 
directive (European Commission, 2014a) suggest a new method for calculation of recycling rate. This 
proposal was annulled in December 2014, but a new more ambiguous proposal might come in 2015 
(DAKOFA, 2015a; European Commission, 2014e). Latest news as of January 2015 is that the amendment 
will be withdrawn, but this has not happened officially yet (DAKOFA, 2015b). The main difference in 
calculation method is where in the value chain the weighing of the material should take place. Currently 
the amount referred to as the recycling rate is the collected amount of plastic waste over the total waste 
generation. However, it is a matter of interpretation where exactly the new measurement should take 
place. The suggestion (European Commission, 2014a, p. 14) reads: 
“For the purpose of calculating whether the targets […] have been achieved, the weight 
of the waste prepared for re-use and recycled shall be understood as the weight of the 
waste which was put into a final preparing for re-use or recycling process less the weight 
of any materials which were discarded in the course of that process due to presence of 
impurities and which need to be disposed of or undergo other recovery operations.”  
In this report it is interpreted that the weighing of the recycled material should take place after 
impurities have been sorted and after the material have been sorted into plastic types. Hence “final 
preparing” is understood as the upgrading process. However, the suggestion could also be interpreted as 
measuring when only impurities have been sorted, i.e. “final preparing” is understood as the sorting 
facility. Impurities are here understood as items, which are not bound to the polymer, e.g. paper and 
metals. The new suggested calculation method implies, if understood as in this report, that the sorting 
efficiency and the amount of impurities in the collected material become more important. If the sorting 
efficiency of the plastic types is not included in the recycling efficiency calculation, this does not give a 
truthful picture, as a large fraction of the plastic is not utilized for recycling, because it cannot be sorted 
into single-polymer flows. 
5.1. 
Methodology and terminology 
Scenario analysis is used to understand possible future pathways of systems. Narrative storylines and a 
quantitative model support the scenarios. The scenarios and used model only include two variables, the 
collection and the sorting efficiency. These are rather simple when compared to other known sets of 
scenarios; for instance the climate change mitigation scenarios from the Intergovernmental Panel on 
Climate Change [IPCC], which are based on complex models with many variables (IPCC, 2014). There are 
usually used two scenario typologies; forecasting and backcasting scenarios. The forecasting scenarios 
begin with the current situation and explore which end-states can be reached assuming different decisions 
are made on the way. The backcasting scenarios, on the other hand, have a fixed, desired or undesired, 
==PAGE:47==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 33 of 110 
end-state and then deduces the pathways to reach the goal (Scenarios for Sustainability, 2015; Vásquez 
and Müller, 2014). 
5.1.1. Storylines 
Three scenarios were developed for mechanical recycling efficiency of Danish household waste plastics as 
well as the benchmarking baseline scenario. The time range is 2014 until 2025, which corresponds to the 
time when Denmark is to reach the long-term European target. The storylines of the scenarios are 
presented below. The aim of creating different scenarios is to understand possible futures of plastic 
recycling efficiencies in Denmark, and to investigate if and how the suggested targets can be reached. 
Two forecasting and one backcasting scenarios were created. 
Baseline scenario assumes no major improvements 
from the situation today. Collection in the 
municipalities grows at a slow rate and the 
collected plastic is continuously sent to primarily 
Germany for sorting and recycling. This scenario is 
built in order to have a benchmarking baseline for 
the other scenarios. 
Forecasting scenario 1 assumes low increase in 
collection and sorting efficiencies. Collection 
efficiency increases as more municipalities begin to 
with kerb side collection, which is possible due to 
political focus. At the same time, there is an 
increase in amounts collected in existing schemes, 
due to continuous information to the citizens. The 
municipalities keep sending the plastic to primarily 
Germany, as no Danish company wants to take the 
risk of opening a plastic recovery facility [PRF]. 
Forecasting scenario 2 considers high increase in 
collection and sorting efficiencies. Collection 
efficiency is assumed to be the same as in 
forecasting scenario 1. Denmark establishes one or 
more efficient recovery facilities increasing the 
sorting efficiencies significantly. This was done as 
companies, organisations and political organs 
cooperate to tackle the existing barriers. The 
facility is built with the most efficient available 
technology. 
Backcasting scenario, which reaches both the mid-
term and long-term targets, assumes as forecasting 
scenario 2 that Denmark establishes recovery 
facilities. What collection efficiency is necessary to 
reach the targets is answered in the results. In 
order to reach the targets a massive effort on 
collection would have to be in place by 2015. This 
requires collection schemes that cover all 
recyclable plastic in all municipalities, immense 
information campaigns, and willingness from all 
citizens. Furthermore, obstacles like carbon black 
plastic are overcome. 
 
5.1.2. Model 
5.1.2.1. Collection efficiency 
The collection efficiency can be affected by multiple parameters, among others the segregation guideline, 
collection service level, engagement from citizens and the information level (Christensen and Matsufuji, 
2010). The collection efficiency is defined as the amount of plastic waste, which is sorted by households 
into the correct container for plastic waste of the total plastic waste generation. It should be noted that 
collection efficiency usually is calculated as a percentage of the material fraction included in the 
segregation guide, i.e. not the whole potential. The collection efficiency is here calculated on the basis of 
the whole plastic waste generation.  
Based on a Swedish investigation in Lund, where 43% of the rigid plastic is collected, Møller et al. (2013) 
estimated an achievable collection efficiency for plastic packaging in Denmark in 2020 of 45% in single 
family house and 25% in multi-family houses. The collection efficiencies for non-packaging plastics were 
==PAGE:48==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 34 of 110 
set to 30% and 15% respectively for single- and multi-family houses. This would give in total a collection 
efficiency of around 30% of the household plastic, assuming that 3/4 of all houses are single-family (round 
off from Danmarks Statistik (2014)) and that around 2/3 of the plastic is packaging. The packaging amount 
is lower than found in Chapter 2, but as argued here, there are many uncertainties tied to the non-
packaging fraction, hence the amount is assumed to be larger than found in current data. Furthermore, it 
is assumed that non-packaging plastic waste will grow in the near future, as a larger share of the plastic 
stock will become obsolete. The highest achievable collection efficiency must be assumed to be similar to 
the one for paper and glass, which has taken many years to achieve. Christensen and Matsufuji (2010) 
reports an efficiency of 90% for full service collection.  Similarly the collection efficiency for paper in 
single family houses was found to be 90% in the report by Møller and colleagues (2013). Compared to 
current plastic collection rates of 10.5%, it is understood that there is a long way to reach these high 
collection efficiencies. 
5.1.2.2. Sorting efficiency 
There are many limitations to the sorting efficiency. The most prevailing  ones are technical challenges as 
current technology cannot sort with 100% efficiency or dismantle complex plastic products, and NIR 
technology cannot sort carbon black coloured plastic (Bigum and Jakobsen, 2014b; Dvorak et al., 2011; 
Nordic Council of Ministers, 2013). Furthermore, the plastic polymers which are not sorted represent a 
major quantity loss. Sorting thus represents one of the major quantity losses in the recycling value chain. 
The sorting efficiency is very dependent on the input material, i.e. the collected material. The sorting 
efficiency covers in consequence what in LCA is determined as the technical substitution coefficient. 
Impurities of non-plastic origin are not included in the terms. 
The City of Copenhagen has performed a comparative assessment of five tests carried out at Danish and 
German sorting facilities (Bigum and Jakobsen, 2014b). The City of Copenhagen has a full service 
collection scheme of rigid plastics. The amount sorted into single-polymers at existing facilities was 33-
43%, and additionally a mixed plastic fraction of 4-27% was sorted out that have an unknown destination. 
These figures correspond to the incoming material excluding the non-plastic impurities, which constituted 
3-8% of the collected material (Bigum and Jakobsen, 2014b). The figures also agree with the sorting 
efficiency found by Villanueva and colleagues (2010) of 38% of all waste plastics intended for recycling in 
Europe being sorted into plastic polymers and 40% to energy recovery. The polymers sorted in the four 
facilities investigated by the City of Copenhagen were PET, HDPE, LDPE and PP. Furthermore, one facility 
sorted PS. These four facilities are assumed to cover the general situation in Europe described in Chapter 
4 on current situation of recycling. The last test was performed at a test facility that achieved a sorting 
efficiency of 63% with the newest NIR technology. It must be mentioned that voluminous fractions were 
excluded from the input at the test facility, which could have affected the results (Bigum and Jakobsen, 
2014b).  
5.1.2.3. Assumptions 
As earlier mentioned the current level of mechanical recycling in Denmark is estimated by The Danish 
Plastics Federation (Clemen, 2014; Lindsø, 2014) to be 10.5% in 2011 for plastic packaging from 
households. It is assumed that this number corresponds to the collected plastic packaging, i.e. following 
the European standard today, which does not cover what is actually recycled. This must be assumed lower 
for the non-packaging waste plastics, referring to assumptions in Møller et al. (2013). The development of 
collection activities in the municipalities have been considerable in recent years, subsequently the total 
collection efficiency for both packaging and non-packaging plastic is assumed to have increased to 12% in 
2014 – an educated guess in order to perform the calculations. 
The baseline scenario assumes an increase of collection of 11% annually, which is based on the annual 
increase in the City of Frederiksberg from 2008 to 2013 (Frederiksberg Kommune, 2014). The first year of 
==PAGE:49==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 35 of 110 
collection was excluded as the increase was more than 50% from 2007 to 2008. The annual increase in 
collection is though still slowing from 15% in 2008 to below 10% in 2013. The sorting efficiency is set to 
38.5%, an average of sorting efficiencies of the four facilities, excluding the mixed plastic, in Bigum and 
Jakobsen (2014b). This efficiency increases linearly to 54% in 2025, which is the average of the four 
facilities including the mixed plastic, i.e. assuming that the technological development can sort all plastic 
currently sorted (both single plastic and mixed plastic) into only single plastic types.  
The first forecasting scenario assumes an intensive effort to include most municipalities, resulting in 
increasing collection efficiency. The increase in collection efficiency is assumed to be 16.50% annually, 
which agrees with the assumption of 30% collection efficiency in 2020 proposed by Møller et al. (2013). 
The sorting efficiency is still set to 38.5% increasing linearly to 54% in 2025 as in the baseline scenario. 
The second forecasting scenario assumes a similar growth as in forecasting scenario 1 in collection 
efficiency. Furthermore, many initiatives on the construction of a waste sorting facility is currently taking 
place from e.g. DAKOFA; hence the scenario assumes that an efficient plastic recovery facility is in place 
in Denmark from 2018 (DAKOFA, 2015c). This increases the sorting efficiency in 2018 to 65% based on the 
results from the test facility (Bigum and Jakobsen, 2014b). The efficiency increases linearly until 70% in 
2025. The increase until 2018 is as in forecasting scenario 1. 
The calculation method (model) for the baseline and forecasting scenarios follows the method suggested 
by the European Commission (European Commission, 2014a):

The backcasting scenario reaches both the mid-term and long-term targets. The proposed European 
targets for plastic packaging are adopted to non-packaging plastics from households in this scenario 
analysis, i.e. mid-term target of 45% in 2020 and long-term target of 60% in 2025. The packaging recycling 
goals for other materials than plastic are higher. The reasoning for not using the targets for recyclates in 
municipal waste for non-packaging plastics is that the target is an overall target for all recyclable 
fractions, i.e. not especially for plastic. The recycling efficiency gains between 2014 and the mid-term 
goal and again between the mid-term and long-term goals are assumed linear. The calculation method 
(model) in the backcasting scenario is:

5.2. 
Results and discussion 
The initial recycling efficiency in 2014 is 4.6%. The baseline scenario reaches a recycling efficiency of 11% 
in 2020 and 20% in 2025, as shown in Figure 5.1. Values of collection, sorting and recycling efficiencies of 
all years can be found in Appendix C. The analysis showed that neither of the two forecasting scenarios 
reaches the targets.  The first forecasting scenario reaches 14% and 35% in 2020 and 2025 respectively. 
Recycling efficiencies of 24% and 45% are reached in 2020 and 2025 in the second forecasting scenario. 
Both forecasting scenarios are closer to reach the long-term target than the short-term one. The 
backcasting scenario shows that collection efficiencies of 68% and 86% are necessary to reach the targets. 
 
==PAGE:50==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 36 of 110 
 
FIGURE 5.1. RESULT OF SCENARIO ANALYSIS. THE RED COLOURS REPRESENT THE COLLECTION EFFICIENCY, THE BLUE 
THE SORTING EFFICIENCY AND THE GREEN THE RESULTING RECYCLING EFFICIENCY. THE LIGHTEST NUANCE REPRESENT 
THE BASELINE AND THE DARKEST THE BACKASTING SCENARIO. 
From the results, it can be concluded that the proposed recycling targets of both mid-term and long-term 
are not possible to meet as the backcasting scenario seem unrealistic. If the current recycling rate 
calculation method is kept, i.e. the collection efficiency is seen as the recycling rate, the forecasting 
scenarios could possibly reach the long-term targets. Nevertheless, it is shown, that this method does not 
show actual amounts recycled. The backcasting scenario meet the targets, but the pathway there seem 
unrealistic. The collection efficiency is very high, and seems unrealistic in current pace of development. 
In 10 years the collection efficiency should increase to the current one of paper and glass, which have 
been fractions sorted during decades. In order to reach the targets all parameters to increase the 
collection efficiency must be in play. 
There are uncertainties tied to the simple model developed. The linear approach to increase in collection 
and sorting efficiencies is unrealistic, but useful for these estimations. As a result, the forecasting 
scenarios might in reality meet the mid-term goal better than here displayed as the annual increase is 
likely higher in introducing years which was the experience in the City of Frederiksberg. Furthermore, the 
targets used cover all packaging, hence do not only cover household plastic packaging, but also 
commercial packaging. The recycling of commercial packaging happens at a higher rate, hence household 
packaging could be compensated by a higher recycling rate in the commercial sector. 
The analysis has looked at plastic as a whole. The actual part of the potential that has a chance to be 
sorted out, simply because the only targeted polymers are PET, HDPE, PP, LDPE and PS, is 79 percent (see 
Chapter 7 Analysis of quality). It is therefore uncertain if the targets could be met, as the total waste 
plastic generation is not recyclable. Furthermore, it is questionable whether the sorting efficiency might 
decrease when collection increases. The reason being an increasing amount of items that are difficult to 
sort would be collected; examples are other plastic types than the five common, plastic items combined

The following chapter will explore the technical limitations to quality in the recycling process. These are 
to be the basis for a quantification of quality of recycled plastic, which will be brought up in Chapter 7 on 
quality. 
 
==PAGE:52==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 38 of 110 
6. Technical limitations to quality in 
upgrading 
The “technical substitution coefficient” has been covered in the previous chapter on scenario analysis, but 
as indicated high collection and sorting efficiencies might compromise the quality of the outcome 
material. There are different definitions of quality in literature, and quantification methods of quality 
have been estimated by few in literature. In LCA of waste management systems the quality loss when 
recovering materials would be covered by the term “market substitution” (Christensen et al., 2010). 
According to Grosso and Consonni (2014) there are two ways to calculate the “market substitution”; 1) by 
considering economical difference between virgin and recycled plastics, or 2) from a technical perspective 
considering material’s properties and the maximum number of recycling cycles. Dall and colleagues (2003) 
define the “theoretical potential” for recycling as the amount of plastic waste excluding the amount of 
plastics, which is not recyclable, due to technical reasons, plastics being reused or hazardous substances 
in the plastic. There is no explanation on the term recyclable, but technical limitations are assumed to be 
the cause, e.g. contaminants which are not hazardous. This term could also cover the quality partly, but 
also includes technical substitution assuming technical reasons include limited recovery. In Table 6.1 
indications of quality losses from different sources and different approaches are observed.  
TABLE 6.1. QUANTIFICATION OF QUALITY IN PLASTIC RECYCLING. 
Plastic type

(economical) [%]

Dall and colleagues (2003) indicate the plastic types have different potentials. PE, PP, PS/EPS is based on 
an evaluation by the Institute of Product Development (IPU) at DTU in 2002 stating that 37% of the 
commercial packaging and 93% of the transport packaging is recyclable (Frees, 2002). The terms 
commercial and transport packaging are used in the Danish packaging supply (Dall et al., 2003). But Frees 
(2002) actually also state that only respectively 14% and 68% would be relevant to recycle considering 
what is technically possible and in high enough quality. Dall and co-workers (2003) therefore use the 
figures lightly. The theoretical potential of PET is based on Danish pilot projects of recycling PET/PE 
bottles, which showed that 80% was suitable for recycling. Due to other commodities than bottles with 
expected lower potential this has been lowered to 70% (Dall et al., 2003), but no further explanation of 
the decrease in recyclability was given. Household plastic waste must therefore be assumed to have a 
lower potential, as these figures are based on all plastic waste including post-industrial plastic waste, 
which are generally assumed to have higher recycling potentials due to more homogenous waste (Joint 
Research Centre - European Commission, 2013a). However, on the other hand the data is outdated, while 
technical developments have increased the potential. Rigamonti and colleagues (2009) calculated the 
market substitution based on economics. The ratio is based on the cost of virgin and recycled film, and is 
then used for both PET and PE. Christensen and colleagues (2010) did not argue for the choice of market 
substitution. 
==PAGE:53==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 39 of 110 
The explanation on recyclability or quality loss is not described in any of the cited reports in Table 6.1. 
Neither is there distinguished in different waste fractions. In Chapter 2 it was found that it is not 
reasonable to assume a single polymer type has a single quality, as there are large differences in the 
properties within the polymer themselves. Furthermore, literature state post-consumer and post-
industrial plastic waste have different qualities (La Mantia, 2002; Villanueva et al., 2010). The low figures 
from Dall an colleagues (2003) compared to the other two sources are assumed to be assigned to inclusion 
of technical substitution in the “theoretical potential”. The economic evaluation of quality only gives an 
indication of the present situation in the market, and the cost is dependent on other factors than the 
quality, most importantly the oil price. Additionally, Rigamonti and colleagues (2009) uses a cost 
difference on film only and then transfers it to the plastic type in general, which is not realistic 
considering the price differences within the plastic types depends on quality. An economical evaluation of 
market substitution can give a good indication, but care has to be taken. The price need to be an average 
price, as there are daily fluctuations in the prices (Plastics News, 2015). And furthermore one should 
carefully choose the quality of recycled plastic to compare to virgin price. 
The limitations to recycling are unanswered and are critical to understand in order to improve the quality 
of plastic recycling. The quality, i.e. the market substitution, depend on multiple parameters similar to 
the technical substitution, which was seen in the scenario analysis in Chapter 5. The current quality of the 
recycled material is also unknown, but an indication of the defined qualities on the market can be found 
by examining technical data sheets of secondary raw material. Analysis of technical data sheets provided 
by recyclers and requirements from manufactures of commodities from recycled plastic showed that 
current quality demands are not detailed and focus on elements like melt flow index, non-plastic 
impurities and process ability (Appendix H). Another indication of quality differences is that the 
applications of virgin and recycled material are considerably different, which was found in Table 2.1. This 
corresponds to the fact that there is a considerable difference between virgin and recycled materials’ 
properties as seen on the example of PET in Table 6.2 below. 
TABLE 6.2. PROPERTIES OF TYPICAL RECYCLED PET COMPARED WITH VIRGIN PET. FROM (HANS ET AL., 2006).

The explanation of the differences lies in the recycling process and gives rise to the questions; what 
causes the decreases and increases in various properties and which limitations are there to retain the 
quality in the material. The limitations in the recycling process, with focus on the upgrading process, are 
investigated in the present chapter. A number of reasons why plastic waste is not applicable for high 
quality recycling are found. The main challenges in the upgrading process have been divided into four 
groups, which may be disadvantageous for the recycling, further manufacturing and/or application 
(Briassoulis et al., 2012; Joint Research Centre - European Commission, 2013a; Stangenberg, 2004; 
Vilaplana and Karlsson, 2008); 
1. Polymer cross contamination. This refers to contamination of the single polymer flow with polymers 
other than the targeted. Contamination stems from design, segregation and sorting , 
2. Additives. Contamination with a vast list of additives from production or manufacture may occur, 
3. Non-plastic impurities. This type of contamination may result from design, service life, segregation, 
collection or sorting. The abbreviated form “impurities” will cover this term in the remainder of the 
report, and 
4. Degradation of polymer. The deterioration of plastics during processing, service life or reprocessing. 
==PAGE:54==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 40 of 110 
6.1. 
Polymer cross contamination 
A number of plastic types make up household waste (Chapter 2), and these are most often collected 
comingled. This constitutes one of the major problems in recycling, as even small amounts of polymeric 
impurities can have great negative effects by radically changing the properties of the recyclate. Virtually 
all polymers are immiscible, with the exception of polyphenylene oxide [PPO] and PS (Daugaard, 2014; La 
Mantia, 2002; Villanueva et al., 2010). The incompatibility of polymers is caused by the difference in 
polarity and melting points. The effects of cross contamination with other polymers are a decline in 
macroscopic properties like adjusted melt flow and decrease in impact strength. The heterogeneous mix 
leads to poor mechanical properties in zones of the polymer matrix. Furthermore, polymer impurities in 
small amounts can burn, if the melting temperatures of the polymers are different, and leave specs, 
affecting visual properties of the final plastic (Briassoulis et al., 2012; Joint Research Centre - European 
Commission, 2013a; La Mantia, 2002; Vilaplana and Karlsson, 2008). There are different techniques to 
determine the composition of in plastic polymers (Vilaplana and Karlsson, 2008). 
When dealing with a polymer mix, compatibilisers are added to reduce the interfacial tension in order for 
the contaminating polymer to be dispersed in the matrix better. Practically all post-consumer plastic 
waste collected comingled will be impossible to sort into single polymer streams, hence compatibilisation 
will most often be a necessity. The compatibility depends on the degradation of the polymers, as polymers 
with higher degradation often will have more end groups which are able to react with the compatibility 
agents, hence the polymers will blend better. There are many commercial compatibilisers on the market 
for common polymer blends. They are generally divided into non-reactive and reactive compatibilisers. 
Non-reactive enhance the adhesion between two polymers as it is miscible with both polymers in the mix, 
e.g. block copolymers. On the other hand the reactive compatibilisers make covalent bonds with the 
polymers, having a functional group which can be grafted to polymers, e.g. reactive maleic anhydride 
[MA] (Daugaard, 2014; La Mantia, 2002; Vilaplana and Karlsson, 2008). The majority of compatibilisers for 
polymer mixes will improve the mechanical properties. Commonly, the compatibility agents constitute 1-
15 % of the new mix, when the secondary polymer constitute typically 10-25 % of the weight (Bertin and 
Robin, 2002; Borovanska et al., 2012; Boufassa et al., 2013; Briassoulis et al., 2013; Calcahno et al., 2008; 
Chand et al., 2007; Coltelli et al., 2004; Elmaghor et al., 2003; Hettema et al., 2002; Kaci et al., 2005; La 
Mantia, 2002; Pawlak et al., 2002; Tao and Mai, 2007; Wu, 2007).  
However, none of these articles on compatibilisation considers recycling of the products made from the 
plastic blends or the application possibilities. It must be assumed the mixes of polymers are virtually 
impossible to separate and mechanically recycle again. Plastic blends should from a circular economy and 
resource preservation perspective be avoided, so that re-recyclability is possible. Furthermore, the 
application possibilities cover a small part of the market demand. Consequently polymer blends cannot be 
determined as high quality recycling.  
Instead, it is interesting to look at the limits for contamination with other polymers for achieving single 
polymer flows instead of polymer mixes, to limit the use compatibilisers. The limits, which can be 
observed in Table 6.3, are in some details presented below the table. In general it was found that 
information regarding a limited amount of contaminating polymer in single polymer streams is missing for 
the plastics other than PET. Existing qualities on the market shows the following is possible: <0.4% 
contamination polymer in PP, <5-200 ppm PVC in PET, <10-100 ppm polyolefins in PET, and <5-200 ppm PA 
in PET (Appendix H). Færch Plast requested for their PET <50ppm in total of other polymers and <10ppm 
PVC. However, this depends on the intended application as they can process up to 1% polyolefin in C-PET 
(Lauridsen, 2014).

The explanations of the immiscibility are given in examples of contaminations in PET here.  
PVC has a lower melt temperature than PET, which means PVC will start to decompose at PET processing 
temperatures. PVC decomposing involves release of hydrochloric acid gas (HCl), which is a very corrosive 
gas that attacks both equipment as well as the PET polymer chains degrading the PET into smaller chains. 
Reduction of chain length means a reduction in intrinsic viscosity. The dehydrochlorination of PVC creates 
a residue char, which affects PET’s optical properties with discoloration and black specs. As low as 50-200 
ppm is enough to deteriorate mechanical and chemical properties (European PET Bottle Platform, 2014; 
Serranti et al., 2012). 
PS have a melt temperature below PET and will negatively affect both mechanical, chemical and optical 
properties (European PET Bottle Platform, 2014). 
Two main types of degradable plastics which are contaminants in PET are described here; biodegradable 
and oxo-degradable. Biodegradable plastics degrades quickly; according to the international standard 
(Dansk standard, 2006) biodegradable plastic must degrade with 90% within 90 days in an industrial 
composting process (Christensen and Fruergaard, 2010). The biodegradable plastic is not compatible with 
PET and assumingly neither with other plastic polymers (European PET Bottle Platform, 2014). An example 
is PLA, which is similar to PET in properties, e.g. the density is above 1 meaning it cannot be separated in 
swim-sink process, but can be sorted with NIR technology at accuracy of 85-96%. Low levels of PLA in PET 
(even lower than 0.1%) will cause deterioration of mechanical and chemical properties. PLA soften and 
become tacky, why flakes stick to the equipment, to each other and to the PET flakes. Large agglomerates 
are formed, which causes manufacture stops and reduce the rate of decontamination process. 
Furthermore, PLA can reduce the intrinsic viscosity of PET and optical properties (European PET Bottle 
Platform, 2014). Another degradable plastic is oxo-degradable plastic, which is enhanced with oxidising 
agents and is not compatible with PET (Briassoulis et al., 2012; European Commission, 2013; European PET 
Bottle Platform, 2014; Joint Research Centre - European Commission, 2013b) and plausible neither with 
other plastic types.  
Separation of glycol-modified PET [PETG] from PET is very difficult as they have similar density and NIR 
technology cannot detect the difference. PETG have a much lower softening temperature than PET, 
similar to PLA it becomes tacky due to softening, and therefore stick to equipment, each other and to the 
PET (European PET Bottle Platform, 2014).  
==PAGE:56==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 42 of 110 
6.2. 
Additives  
Additives are added to virgin polymers to improve different properties of the product or process ability 
during the production or manufacturing. During the service life some of these additives might go through 
chemical transformation or migrate (Vilaplana and Karlsson, 2008). The chemically transformed additive 
and additives that stay intact will accumulate in the regranulate as they cannot be removed, leading to 
decreased quality compared to virgin plastic and thus downcycling is a reality. This is evaluated as a major 
obstacle for plastic recycling by the European Commission (2013). Quantifying and identifying the 
different compounds is possible, but difficult and oftentimes not performed (Vilaplana and Karlsson, 
2008), as each batch is different requiring continued testing and additionally the many different additives 
would require many individual tests. The quantity and composition of additives varies depending on basic 
properties of the polymer and application. In PVC up to 50-60% of the plastic could be additives and PVC 
consumes about one-third of all plasticisers and heat stabilisers used in plastics (Murphy 2001 in Joint 
Research Centre - European Commission, 2013b). In total consumption of additives by volume polyolefins 
follow with 10% and styrenics consume 5%, where in PET bottles the additives are more often less than 1% 
of the weight (ibid.). The most common additive group are plasticisers constituting up to 20-60% in PS and 
PVC, whereas other additives like stabilisers is 1-5% of the weight (Daugaard, 2014; Joint Research Centre 
- European Commission, 2013a). The additives found by the largest mass in a plastic product will be 
presented first and then in decreasing order. The plastic additives are not limited to the ones presented 
here. 
6.2.1. Fillers and reinforcing agents 
Fillers could be added up to 70% of the weight in order to increase the bulk of a polymer. The use of fillers 
is common as they are cheaper than polymer and may reduce significantly the cost of the final compound 
Examples of filler and reinforcing agents are glass fibres, wood fibres, calcium carbonate and talc. They 
commonly alter density making the sorting process more difficult where sink-swim technologies are used. 
Fillers are often added in the recycling process to improve mechanical properties (Briassoulis et al., 2012; 
Cuculiza, 2014; Joint Research Centre - European Commission, 2013a).  
6.2.2. Plasticisers 
Plasticisers dominate the market of synthetic additives, but the growth is slow (Joint Research Centre - 
European Commission, 2013a), likely because of slowing increase in PVC consumption. Plasticisers stabilize 
the polymer chains, while lowering the softening range and increase the mobility (Dansk standard, 2013). 
Plasticisers affect the glass transition temperature, hence processing has to happen at higher 
temperatures. Plasticisers are not chemically bound to the polymer matrix, making them susceptible to 
migration and partial degradation thorough hydrolysis. Commonly used plasticisers have been classified as 
endocrine disrupting substances with human health concerns being voiced in the literature (Kamrin, 2009). 
No indications on how plasticisers affect recycling were found. 
6.2.3. Stabilisers 
Stabilisers can be up to 4% of weight of plastic and hinder the degradation of polymers due to e.g. 
oxidation or exposure to UV light. The lack of them in polyolefins lowers elongation at break, impact 
strength and stiffness probably due to degradation. Losses of stabilisers during the service life can be 
compensated by new, which prevents further degradation, but does not restore polymer length. Around 
40% of all antioxidants and light stabilisers is used in polypropylene (Joint Research Centre - European 
Commission, 2013a; La Mantia, 2002; Vilaplana and Karlsson, 2008). UV stabilisers does according to the 
European PET Bottle Platform (2014) make recycling conditional, but there is no elaboration on reason. 
According to La Mantia (2002) transformation products of processing and long term stabilisers also affect 
the recycled plastic negatively, while no explanation is given here neither.  
==PAGE:57==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 43 of 110 
6.2.4. Pigments and dyes 
Colouring of plastic imparts an often times undesirable grey colour to the recycled plastic, if the plastics 
is not colour separated. Colour is one of the parameters which are often mentioned in the context of 
limitations to recycling (Briassoulis et al., 2012; Dansk standard, 2007a; Villanueva et al., 2010). There are 
a number of different pigments and dyes, e.g. metals compounds are also used as colorants, like Pb and 
Cd (European Commission, 2006). The pigment titanium dioxide amount to 4-8% of the weight in PVC 
window frames (Joint Research Centre - European Commission, 2013a) and assumingly there are similar 
amounts of pigments and dyes in other coloured plastics. Colour separation mainly happens to sort out 
transparent PET bottles, which have a wide range of applications and thereof a high market value. At 
times transparent light-blue are intentionally mixed in to cover undesirable yellowing caused by 
degradation. The presence of opaque colours reduce the value significantly (European PET Bottle 
Platform, 2014).  
Recently the recyclability of black plastic, namely plastic coloured with carbon black, has been discussed.  
The reason is that the commonly implemented sorting technology NIR cannot sort the black plastic into 
types. This is because the infrared light is absorbed by carbon black instead of being reflected. Other 
sorting techniques are available (e.g. Mid Infrared [MIR] and Raman Laser spectroscopy) which can sort 
carbon black, but they are not commercially available yet (Dvorak et al., 2011). WRAP concluded in a 
recent study that on the short and medium time scale using alternative black colours detectable by NIR 
would potentially be the best solution (Briassoulis et al., 2012; Dvorak et al., 2011; Joint Research Centre 
- European Commission, 2013a). 
6.2.5. Barriers 
Barrier properties are especially interesting in the context of beverages and food, e.g. in PET bottles. PET 
has good barrier properties, but still there is a growing demand for enhanced barrier properties. This is 
explained by a trend towards light weight packaging, enhanced product performance and shelf life, at the 
same time as expanding the applications to beer, juice, dairy and wine, highly carbonated and/or oxygen-
sensitive drinks (European PET Bottle Platform, 2014). There are different technologies to enhance barrier 
properties; either monolayer or multilayer. A monolayer is where additives are blended with the polymer 
(e.g. Nylon-MXD6 carrier). On the other hand multilayer is either an external/internal coating on the 
bottle surface (e.g. SiOx, carbon, epoxy) or a “sandwich” with 3 or 5 layers (e.g. MXD5 or EVOH). 
Additionally, barriers are divided into passive and active, where a passive barrier prevents a gas or liquid 
to transfer and an active barrier reacts with the gas. Combinations are also seen. Typically, the barrier 
material is less than 5% of the weight. Identifying the intact bottles with barrier material is not possible. 
The barrier material should therefore be compatible with PET, or the other polymer. The barrier agents 
which is a middle layer without tie layers is easier to separate from the PET bottles, but only if the plastic 
waste is shredded before identification. All the barrier materials, except SiOx coating causes 
discoloration, yellowing or haze in PET (European PET Bottle Platform, 2014).
