6.2.6. Miscellaneous 
 
Acetaldehyde [AA] blockers, reduces the AA which is a natural chemical in some foodstuff and is 
produced in PET production. AA affects the flavour of food (European PET Bottle Platform, 2014; 
Pack4recycling, 2015), 
 
Residues of polymerisation catalysts (La Mantia, 2002), 
 
Nano-particles, commonly nano clays, tubes, sillicon dioxide and titanium dioxide in waste plastic 
(Andersen et al., 2014). The nano clays and tubes are not compatible with PET and can constitute 1-
5% of weight and colour the plastic black (European PET Bottle Platform, 2014), and  
 
Flame retardants increase fire resistance of plastics. Mostly relevant for non-packaging plastics with 
higher risks of fire (i.e. furniture, electric and electronic equipment). Conventional flame retarding 
chemicals were classified as persistent environmental pollutants, which resulted in limitations of their 
use (Shaw, 2010). 
6.3. 
Non-plastic impurities 
Non-plastic impurities have their source from the design with combined materials (i.e. composites), from 
the use phase and waste mis-sorting. Non-polymeric impurities affect mainly optical properties and 
process ability. Removing these impurities requires (pre-)washing and pre-sorting (Briassoulis et al., 2012). 
The newly released End-of-Waste criteria sets a limit of 2% of non-plastic impurities for recycled plastic 
(Villanueva and Eder, 2014). In the recycling industry today values for PET bottles of <25ppm to <1000ppm 
were found in technical data sheets (Appendix H). Specific values were also defined on single impurities, 
e.g. metals (<5; <20 ppm), paper (<5; <20 ppm), glue (<30 ppm), multilayer flakes (<200 ppm), flakes w. 
label (<400 pm), flakes w. glue (<1000ppm) and dust (<0.1, <0.5%). 
6.3.1. Metals 
Even very small amounts of metals can cause detrimental effects on quality and affect the processing of 
plastics. Particles of metal can block filters and injection nozzles and furthermore, affect optical 
properties (European PET Bottle Platform, 2014). 
6.3.2. Label adhesives 
Label adhesives, which are not water- or alkali-soluble at temperatures of 60-80°C (conditions commonly 
employed in plastic waste washing step) will be transferred to the reprocessing, causing discoloration and 
loss of clarity (European PET Bottle Platform, 2014; Shah, 1998). 
==PAGE:59==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 45 of 110 
6.3.3. Ink 
Inks are a mixture of many components, like colorants and binding agents. Inks are transferred to the 
reprocessing via labels and sleeves, which have not been sorted. Labels and sleeves in the same material 
as the plastic product will especially be transferred because sorting is difficult. In addition, print directly 
on the packaging will be transferred. Furthermore, inks can bleed during caustic washing, which can 
colour the flakes. Inks will impact the optical properties of the recycled plastic (European PET Bottle 
Platform, 2014). Many guidelines on recyclability have been published by the European Printing Ink 
Association (EuPIA, 2015). 
6.3.4. Paper 
Paper originates from labels and incorrectly sorted waste. If paper start to pulp in the washing process, 
this can cause the paper to stick to flakes and be carried over to the upgrading process. The paper will 
start to burn in the extrusion and cause black specs or microscopic holes (pinholes) in the plastic 
(European PET Bottle Platform, 2014). 
6.3.5. Miscellaneous 
Items known to create difficulties in the recycling process; 
 
Radio frequency identification [RFID] tags are increasing in quantity and often attached to packaging 
with strong adhesive, which makes them difficult to sort out. The tags consists of metal, plastic and 
paper (European PET Bottle Platform, 2014). An investigation by Aliaga and colleagues (2011) showed 
that the quality (measured on five mechanical, one thermal and one optical property) did not 
decrease with increasing amount of RFID tags, but the process efficiency decreased, 
 
Organic matter contamination beyond 3-5% by weight is not acceptable according to (Briassoulis et al., 
2012), which compared to the earlier mentioned 2% in the EoW criteria seem high, 
 
Fatty acids, oils, paints (La Mantia, 2002) and  
 
Færch Plast experienced that dust can cause unexplained problems in the decontamination process 
(Lauridsen, 2014). The dust might partly be nano particles explaining the incompatibility with PET.  
6.4. 
Degradation of polymers 
Degradation can happen both during the service life of the product with exposure to environmental 
conditions, commonly ultra violet radiation (photo-degradation), or during (re-)processing due to 
mechanical and thermal forces (thermo-mechanical degradation). Degradation does not solely depend on 
the polymer type, as the same polymer of different applications are in different degradation stages 
related to different service lifetime. The concentration of new structures/chemical changes in the 
polymer is dependent on the oxidation sensibility and intensity and duration of ageing and it is possible to 
measure these changes in different ways (Vilaplana and Karlsson, 2008).  
There are many different forms of degradation of polymers e.g. mechanical, thermal, UV-light and 
weathering. Heat, light or weathering generally result in degradation through three main mechanisms: 
random chain scission, chain-end depolymerisation or side-group cleavage (actually a subset of random 
chain scission). Some polymers are more prone to degrade by one mechanism than the other and generally 
polyolefins are considered to degrade by random-chain scission, whereas other polymers such as PMMA and 
PTFE mainly degrade through chain-end depolymerisation. PS on the other hand is an example of a 
polymer that degrades through an intermediary mechanism that has character of both chain-end and 
random scission. Side-group cleavage is generally a degradation phenomena observed for PVC (Daugaard, 
2014). 
==PAGE:60==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 46 of 110 
As a consequence of thermo-mechanical degradation caused by (re-)processing the molecular weight 
decrease and with increasing processing steps an increase in melt flow rate [MFR], but this phenomenon is 
dependent on polymer type, which is sketched in Figure 6.1 (Vilaplana and Karlsson, 2008). A consequence 
of decrease in molecular weight is discoloration or micro-cracks. 
 
FIGURE 6.1. DEVELOPMENT OF MELT FLOW RATE [MFR] 
WITH INCREASING PROCESSING CYCLES. NOT IN SCALE. 
(VILAPLANA AND KARLSSON, 2008). 
Degradation in the use phase happens as the stabilisers are consumed; leading to a progressive reduction 
in polymer length as UV can attack an increasing number of places in the chain, and to formation of 
functional groups like hydroxyl, carbonyl, carboxylic and ether groups. Other functional groups will be 
consumed as a consequence of degradation, like ester linkage in PET (Briassoulis et al., 2012; 
Stangenberg, 2004; Villanueva et al., 2010). This leads to chain scission, i.e. the breakage of the chemical 
bonds on the polymer chains, lowering the molecular weight. Crosslinking also occurs affecting the 
rheology, as well as increase the molecular weight. This happens at a molecular level; affecting molecular 
structure, long term stability, rheological and mechanical properties (Briassoulis et al., 2012; Vilaplana 
and Karlsson, 2008; Vilaplana et al., 2007). Most mechanical properties decrease, but some like stiffness 
could increase. Mechanical properties affected is also different depending on plastic type; changes in 
elastic modulus for PP but increased crystallinity in semicrystalline polymers (LDPE, HDPE, C-PET) 
(Vilaplana and Karlsson, 2008). Rheological changes, i.e. a decrease in viscosity and an increase in 
crystallinity, create different chain-length showing that there is a range of melting peaks which is similar 
to what is observed in mixed plastic, whereas virgin plastic show just one melt peak. Plastics with 
multiple melt peaks is more difficult to process (Stangenberg, 2004; Vilaplana and Karlsson, 2008).  
Simulated recycling has been performed by Jansson et al. (2004) and Luzuriaga et al. (2006) where both 
degradation steps (photo degradation in use-phase and thermo-mechanical degradation in reprocessing) 
were performed in combination resulting in an accelerated ageing. Consequently, degradation happens 
faster in recyclates than in virgin material. The cause of accelerated degradation is assumed to be the 
formation of new functional groups which increase the sensitivity towards thermal- and photo-degradation 
(La Mantia, 2002; Vilaplana and Karlsson, 2008). Testing the thermo-mechanical and thermo-oxidative 
degradation separately PET, HDPE and PP show that thermo-mechanical degradation affects the oxidation 
induction time [OIT] most  (a measurement for thermal stabilization level) (Vilaplana and Karlsson, 2008), 
where it is opposite for styrenic polymers like PS (Vilaplana et al., 2006). 
==PAGE:61==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 47 of 110 
In order to upgrade by restoring properties in the polymer, the chemical structure needs to be rebuild, 
which will increase the molecular weight and improve mechanical and rheological properties. Rebuilding 
the chemical structures is only possible in poly-condensation polymers, like PET, where the existing chains 
are extended with additives that react with the hydroxyl and carbonyl end groups (Awaja and Pavel, 
2005). In polyolefins and other addition-polymers additives with different functional groups are added 
which creates branching and cross-linking (La Mantia and Tzankova Dintcheva, 2005; Scaffaro et al., 2007, 
2006).  
6.5. 
Heterogeneous input material 
The chapter have shown there are many parameters affecting properties, i.e. affecting the quality of the 
recycled material. The contamination of the input material to the upgrading process is very determining 
for the output material. The input material to the upgrading process is heterogeneous when it comes to 
household plastic. It can therefore be concluded that the processes prior to upgrading are essential to 
determine the input material; e.g. the composition of consumption, production, manufacture, segregation 
guideline and the possibility at the sorting facility. There are different characterisation factors, which the 
material can be characterised by, which have been sketched in Figure 6.2. The figure shows the five 
polymers types and the four different contamination types. There are many possible pathways for the 
individual polymer. Consequently, there are many different possible qualities of recycled household 
plastic waste as the polymers can be contaminated to different extents and with different 
contaminations. The limits of low contamination are the limit for when high quality are achievable, 
whereas the medium contamination level will correspond to a lower quality output. The limit for polymer 
cross contamination is e.g. estimated to be below 1% of the input material to the upcycling process in 
order to consider the output as high quality materials. The limits here given are not polymer dependent, 
hence the figure only gives a general picture of all polymers, e.g. presumably cross contamination in HDPE 
can be higher than in PET. These limits also assumes that the best available technology is applied in the 
upcycling process. The main conclusions will be drawn from each of the four categories of contamination. 
It will be discussed which contaminations can be handled and which cannot. The properties affected by 
the contamination and in addition, the contaminant’s dependence on polymer type will be discussed.  
==PAGE:62==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 48 of 110 
 
FIGURE 6.2. GRADES OF INPUT MATERIAL FOR THE UPGRADING PROCESS BASED ON LIMITATIONS TO QUALITY. THE 
ARROWS SHOW AN EXAMPLE OF THE PATH FOR HIGH QUALITY PET. LIMITS MIGHT BE DEPENDENT ON POLYMER AND 
THE LIMITS HERE ARE GENERAL TO GIVE A PICTURE OF DIFFERENT PATHWAYS OF THE PLASTIC. 
There is a general awareness of recyclers on polymer cross contamination. Cross contamination affects 
mechanical, optical and chemical properties. The type of contaminating polymer is important, as the 
polymers are more or less incompatible with other polymers, e.g. PVC has lower limits than other 
polymers. It seems like PET is more sensitive to contamination compared to other polymers. However, this 
might be because PET in general has a higher quality and value, so the extra sorting and upgrading is 
worth-while. In general, there is a greater knowledge regarding PET. An estimation of maximum 1% 
contamination of other polymers are set in order to reach high quality plastics, based on the possibilities 
of C-PET at Færch Plast. The medium limit is set based on data from Aage Vestergaard Larsen for HDPE. 
These limits are dependent on polymer type. This consequently means that contaminating polymers like 
PVC and degradable plastics must not be present in the waste stream, because they have to be present at 
very low levels which is likely not technically possible achieve, i.e. be excluded in the segregation 
guidelines. Polymers with high contamination levels (<4%) will need compatibilisers.  
The recycling industry’s awareness regarding additives is low and there are in general missing knowledge 
regarding the subject. The effects on quality of e.g. plasticisers and stabilisers in recycling are unknown. 
It is known that certain barrier agents and monolayer barrier mixes are not desirable. The properties 
known to be affected are optical and mechanical properties. Furthermore, knowledge regarding the 
dependence on polymer is missing. It is known that there is a difference in the amount of additives in 
different polymers; PET have less additives compared to polyolefins and PS.  The limit for content of 
additives to acquire a high quality plastic is unknown and should be investigated further. The limit could 
be based on amount of additive, but also the number of different additives might be of importance. Some 
additives might even be acceptable without in the recycling process, e.g. SiOx plasma coating as a barrier 
agent. 
A limit of 2% of non-plastic contamination is recommended in the European Commission’s End-Of-Waste 
[EoW] criteria (Villanueva and Eder, 2014). Comparing this limit to existing qualities on the PET bottle 
market, it is found that a lower limit might be necessary in order to improve the quality, assuming that 
PET bottle recycling is usually recycled into higher quality products. Therefore the limit is estimated to be 
1000ppm, which is the highest acceptable value found in technical data sheets. The medium is set to 2% 
==PAGE:63==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 49 of 110 
found by EoW criteria. The awareness in industry is good on the matter of impurities. Properties affected 
are process ability and optical. The non-plastic impurities are not polymer dependent, but there might be 
a difference in the amount of impurities in the input material depending on source and product category. 
There is some awareness to degradation in industry, but it is often not viewed as a problem. An example is 
that Aage Vestergaard Larsen rarely have problems caused by degradation (Cuculiza, 2014). The 
explanation is that the effect of degradation will not be detectable until a number of recycling cycles, 
which currently rarely happen, because cross contamination, impurities or additives will prevent the 
plastic to be recycled again. Currently degradation is less problematic compared to the other 
contaminations. To determine the degree of degradation will be important when recycling is more 
established and materials will make several cycles before being discarded (Vilaplana and Karlsson, 2008). 
Furthermore, degradation in the use phase is not as important for short-lived packaging compared to 
products that have a longer lifetime and is exposed to the environment to a greater extent. Degradation 
during (re-)processing is though still present. The properties affected are mechanical, optical, thermal 
and process ability. The limit to degradation cannot be determined, but the unit could be length of 
polymers or cycles of recycling. The dependence on polymers is present, e.g. PET is less affected by the 
degradation because the polymers can be extended again. 
Prioritising the four contamination areas concerning which are the essential at the moment and which are 
rather essential in the future will be discussed here.  
 
Additives. The category currently has the largest knowledge gap, with unknown effects in the 
recycled plastic. Additives are added in the production and manufacture phase indicating where in 
the value chain the focus need to be in order to improve the quality.  
 
Polymer cross contamination. Since there is still lacking knowledge especially regarding single 
polymer recycling, which needs to be investigated further.  
 
Non-plastic impurities. 
 
Degradation. Because degradation would likely not be of importance before recycling of a 
material happens multiple times.  
It is observed that quality not only depend on macroscopic properties (such as colour, shape and visible 
non-plastic impurities) and melt flow index, which are currently emphasised in technical data sheets of 
recycled plastic. Between the recyclers and the manufactures of new plastic products, lies a great 
information gap, as manufacturers set low requirements to the recycling sector. This consequently means 
primarily low quality products are produced from recycled plastic, i.e. downcycling. The missing link for 
quality improvement in recycling household plastics today is that the manufacturers of the products 
should see a benefit in challenging the recycling sector by setting appropriate requirements to the quality 
of the recycled material. FORCE argues it is possible and that the right requirements just have to be set in 
order to perform the right tests to improve quality. Traceability of the substances in the plastic limits the 
possibilities of applications, hence using plastics in certain product, e.g. food packaging, would require 
too many analysis of the material due to heterogeneously. The waste plastics are a blend of unknown 
composition, which is also the reason for plastic product manufacturer to be reluctant of the use. Some of 
the contaminants are not possible to remove hence recovering the properties in the recycling process is 
not possible. Looking back the limitations discussed in here will be used in the following chapter to define 
quality quantitative and to determine where in the earlier processes improvements are to be suggested.  
==PAGE:64==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 50 of 110 
7. Analysis of quality  
This chapter has four main aims. The first is to discuss the impact of existing quality criteria, which has 
the purpose of improving quality. Secondly, possible quality improvements in the value chain are looks 
into. Thirdly, improvements are quantified to analyse the current, possible and future quality of recycled 
plastic from households. Lastly, a quality hierarchy is proposed.  
7.1. 
Existing quality criteria 
In this subchapter, examples of the most prevailing standards, the End of Waste [EoW] criteria and design 
guidelines will be described. They are all voluntary agreement with the purpose of improving quality in 
recycling. They are reviewed in the context of the findings in Chapter 6 and the definition of quality.  
Loss of information and traceability is an often-discussed limitation to improved quality. According to the 
standard EN 15343 “Plastics – Recycled Plastics – Plastics recycling traceability and assessment of 
conformity and recycled content” (Dansk standard, 2007b) there are two options when traceability is not 
present, which is most often the situation with post-consumer waste. The first option is to control or sort 
the incoming material to a degree that guarantees contaminants removal. In order to control the input 
material the collection and segregation schemes have to be designed appropriately to the recycling 
technologies available, also considering the markets for the recycled plastic. The second option is that 
recyclers get a qualified process for removing the contaminants to such a level that they do not affect the 
intended application of the recyclate. This also means that some plastic producers will not accept 
recycled plastics, because the history of the material is unknown and potential hazardous substances 
could be present (Harper, 1996).   
There are a number of European standards on recycled plastics, which all have status as Danish standards. 
There is a general standard and one on each of the following plastic types: PET, PE, PS, PVC and PP (EN 
15342 – 15348) (Appendix D). The standards follow the second option to cope with traceability, i.e. the 
recycling process must be able to remove contaminants to a level that “do not affect the intended 
application” (Dansk standard, 2007c, p. 4). Possible contaminants are undefined and the level 
unquantified. The standards have a number of required and optional properties, which the recycler must 
or could provide to the buyer of the plastic. Comparing these European standards to each other there are 
large differences in the requirements, which have no immediate explanation. The only parameters that 
are required in all standards are colour and shape. Additionally, there are two properties (particle size 
and melt flow index) which are required in all except one standard. In total there are 34 different 
properties mentioned in the six standards. There are five to seven required properties and between three 
and eleven optional properties in each of the standards. The standards cover partially optical, 
mechanical, thermal and physical properties, but not chemical or weathering properties. The standards 
contain limited information, primarily on impurities of non-plastic items and cross-contamination. In 
addition, they do not cover degradation in the polymers and only the standard for PS covers additives with 
one optional requirement. Furthermore, there are no recommended limits to the impurities, but only a 
requirement to inform about the levels. The use of the standards is not widespread in Denmark. None of 
the companies spoken to had knowledge of their existence (Borggaard, 2014; Cuculiza, 2014; Lauridsen, 
2014). At the same time their technical data sheets almost lived up to the standards. It would seem like 
the standards were already in place. Hence using the standards would most likely not increase the 
information level neither the quality of the output.  
The newly released End of Waste [EoW] criteria contains requirements for the input, the treatment 
processes, the management system and the output of the recovery facility. The input must not come from 
the following sources; hazardous waste, bio-waste, health care waste or personal hygiene products.  In 
==PAGE:65==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 51 of 110 
the treatment processes the incoming waste plastic streams shall be kept separate, e.g. the criteria 
recommend avoiding shredding. There is a requirement to perform a number of processes not specified 
entirely in order to create a free flowing output, which can be fed to manufacturing of new plastic 
products. The management system shall include monitoring of quality, treatment processes and 
techniques, acceptance control, feedback from customers, record keeping of the results, review and 
improvement of the management system as well as training of the staff. The management system shall be 
certified. Furthermore, the EoW criteria do not define quality even when the main purpose of the report 
is to set criteria for End-of-Waste to improve the quality. The requirements for the output material are 
that the facility shall use the European standards (EN 15342-15348) earlier described (Dansk standard, 
2007a, 2007b, 2007c, 2007d, 2007e, 2007f, 2007g), which was concluded not to increase the quality 
significantly. According to the criteria, the material shall furthermore have a limit of 2% non-plastic 
components, which is defined as materials that are not integral components of the plastic structure, e.g. 
fillers is not included in the 2%. Furthermore, in compliance with the existing hazardous regulation, the 
waste plastic must be free of leachable fluids like oil, glue and solvents (Joint Research Centre - European 
Commission, 2013b; Villanueva and Eder, 2014). Comparing the EoW criteria to the limitations found in 
Chapter 6, non-plastic contamination is the only of the four categories which is addressed. Polymer cross-
contamination, additives and degradation are not touched upon. It is however an improvement from the 
standards as there is an actual limit to the non-plastic contamination. The EoW criteria for metals have 
after less than two months in practice improved quality in recycled metal according to recyclers 
themselves. The improvements regards a strict rule of maximum contamination and introduction of quality 
management systems (Saveyn et al., 2014), i.e. similar to the end of waste criteria of plastic. It is 
however questionable if the 2% limit can be regarded as a strict limit to plastic recycling. 
There are a number of design guidelines for improved recyclability, e.g. WRAP looks at recyclability of 
plastic packaging (Foster et al., 2013) and the EU Life+ project, Plastic Zero, also made a design guideline 
(Plastic ZERO, 2015). The European PET Bottle Platform [EPBP] is another, which looked at the 
requirements for mechanical recycling in Europe for post-consumer PET bottles and made a design 
guideline to improve recyclability into high quality products (European PET Bottle Platform, 2014). The 
EPBP guideline has been chosen to elaborate on as it was viewed as the most technically detailed. The 
design guidelines look at different steps in the value chain compared to the standards and EoW criteria, 
i.e. the design, production and manufacture processes. The EPBP guideline focused on additives, polymer 
cross-contamination and non-plastic impurities, but does not include degradation. The latter might be 
explained by the fact that PET degrades slowly and it is possible to prolong the polymer chains again and 
is thereof not viewed as a limitation to recycling PET bottles. The EPBP operates with four key principles; 
1) avoiding materials known to hinder recycling or reduce quality, 2) reducing the amount of non-PET 
components to ease sorting, 3) designing components to be easily separated from the PET and 4) avoiding 
compromise with product safety. They operate with three levels of compatibility; full, limited and low, on 
14 parameters. Furthermore, the design guideline is split into clear/light blue PET bottles and coloured 
PET bottles. The knowledge from the design guidelines is assumed to be transferable knowledge to other 
plastic types and product groups.  
It is not enough to look at one or two steps in the value chain which the above mentioned existing quality 
criteria do. The quality criteria have to be applied to all steps in the value chain. Furthermore, the 
criteria above do not cope with all of the limitations to quality found in Chapter 6. All contaminants 
cannot be removed in a recycling process; hence certain contaminants need to be avoided. The found 
limitations originated from different stages in the value chain. The current voluntary agreements do not 
increase quality. The standards and EoW criteria are rather a picture of the current situation. If design 
guidelines were followed carefully and widespread, in order to get a larger volume, it might be able to 
improve the quality in recycling. This would require work to set design criteria for other plastic polymers, 
which would have to look at the entire value chain.  
==PAGE:66==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 52 of 110 
7.2. 
Quality assessment 
The aim of this assessment is to reveal the limiting processes in the value chain in regards to quality. 
Quality losses can be assigned either to the polymers basic properties or to contaminations in the value 
chain. The contamination categories which are dependent upon polymers are additives and degradation. 
Additives are added to different degrees in polymers and degradation happens dependently upon the 
chemical structure. These will be addressed later. Drawing upon knowledge from earlier chapters, 
especially Chapter 2 and 6, it is found that contamination and possible improvements of contamination 
are to be found in the entire value chain, except extraction. This has been sketched in Table 7.1 below. 
From this it is clear that if better quality is wanted in plastic recycling, the whole value chain needs to be 
addressed. This was also the conclusion from the evaluation of the existing quality criteria. 
TABLE 7.1. CONTAMINATION SOURCES (C) AND IMPROVEMENT POTENTIALS (I) OF THE FOUR LIMITATIONS ACROSS THE

The assessment approach for understanding the needs and the requirements for high quality mechanical 
recycling of plastic was to look at the value chain in reverse order. Three time levels are considered; 
current situation, possible improvement with current technologies and lastly, suggestions for the 
necessary future changes in order to improve the quality. The assessment includes: 
 
Firstly, an investigation of the properties, which the manufacturing need to have requirements to, 
if the secondary raw material should replace virgin raw material. This will map the needs of 
different plastic types and commodities, which is essential to fulfil in order to have high quality 
recycling.  
 
Secondly, it is investigated what these property requirements will set of demands to the previous 
processes in the value chain considering the four limitation categories (polymer cross 
contamination, additives, non-plastic impurities and degradation). 
 
Thirdly, the quality losses are calculated considering the three time levels (current, possible and 
future) and the findings are discussed. 
7.2.1. Secondary material requirements 
In this section, an investigation of the consumption of plastics in households is performed to determine 
the industrial needs in order to use more recycled household plastics. The applications differ greatly, as 
presented in Figure 7.1 which shows the estimated consumption of plastics divided into different grades of 
commodities and plastic types. The commodity groups are similar in applications demands, hence the 
choice of division. Each commodity group would therefore have similar demands of properties, i.e. the 
same quality need. This will be described later. Another argument for the division into applications is that 
it will ease communication to citizens for the municipalities.

collected in municipal plastic schemes, i.e. 49% of the potential is excluded. Chemical packaging is 
assumed to cover commodities like dishwasher soap and vinegar as well as stronger chemicals. Examples 
of the other packaging commodities can be observed in Table 2.2; the two largest being packaging for

that there was no LDPE in the plastic waste, but this could be assigned to a definition difference of 
packaging, as there was a large fraction of foils and bags. Non-packaging plastic products cover a range of 
commodities as described in theory (Chapter 2). These commodities cover a range of plastic types, as 
observed in Figure 2.2, where the largest fraction is plastics other than the five common types. 
In general, the actual properties need to be determined in order to group the plastic in greater detail. 
The manufacturers of plastic products oftentimes have little knowledge of the actual needs, which can be 
concluded by the low details in technical specifications from manufactures. The plastic producers 
generally do not supply information about the formulation of their products such as the content and type 
of stabiliser, processing aid etc. Such information is only available for the higher purity grades that are 
being sold for e.g. the medico industry, where legislation puts requirements on the processers to 
document high purity of their products. In these cases the formulation may be given under confidentiality 
and is not necessarily available as part of the documentation for a product, which generally results in the 
knowledge of the virgin plastic composition being low in both manufacture and recycling industries 
(Daugaard, 2014). The product groups, described in Figure 7.1, are assumed to have similar property 
demands, which are estimated in Table 7.2.

The physical properties are important to all commodity groups. Mechanical properties are essential in all 
commodities. Chemical properties are especially important for food and chemical packaging, because of 
health considerations and barrier needs. This also means increased levels of additives (barrier agents). 
Furthermore, both food and chemical packaging cause non-plastic contamination due to their 
applications. Thermal properties are important to all commodity groups, as especially the process ability 
is dependent upon this. Weathering properties are especially significant to non-packaging commodities 
due to higher degradation exposure with their longer service life and possible outdoor application. 
Differently packaging has short lifetime, i.e. less exposure to degradation. Optical properties are 
important to all product groups. Finally, legislation dictates that food packaging cannot be replaced by 
anything but food grade approved plastic hence it can only be recycled within the product group. This will 
be described further below. Most likely it is possible to replace plastic from other grades in non-packaging 
plastic products, except that there might also be legislative restrictions to the use of recycled plastics 
apart from the food grade products, e.g. in toys, cars, safety equipment and durable products for other 
uses. 
==PAGE:69==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 55 of 110 
7.2.1.1. Food approved packaging 
Looking at Figure 7.1 it is observed that 56% of the total consumption is food grade approved plastics. It is 
technically possible to use 100% recycled PET [R-PET] in food packaging. The limit in legislation is that at 
least 95% of the R-PET must be food-approved. Færch Plast uses up to 85% R-PET in their C-PET 
manufacture and up to 90% in their A-PET manufacture (Lauridsen, 2014). The recycled PET is merely from 
one source of waste; clear PET bottles, which are made from A-PET (Appendix H). Færch Plast adds three 
modifiers to A-PET to crystallize the PET to C-PET. This is done in order to manufacture certain types of 
applications, and it will furthermore improve the processability. The additives are a polyolefin, a virgin 
co-modifier and a stabilizer for the polyolefin. Unidentified problems with the C-PET manufacture from R-
PET occurred few times (Lauridsen, 2014). The additives could make the following recycling difficult due 
to polymer cross contamination. Looking at the technical data sheet of the properties, Færch Plast only 
requires there are test results of pH, intrinsic viscosity, moisture content and colour. The homogenous 
waste source is of utmost importance to define the mechanical, chemical and thermal properties. 
Furthermore, requirements for non-plastic and cross polymer impurities are well defined in the technical 
data sheet (Appendix H). There are no requirements of information of additives or degradation state, but 
the latter is monitored during processing (Lauridsen, 2014). It is a success story with PET bottles, because 
of the large amount of similar molecular structure and few additives. There is little knowledge regarding 
recycling food approved plastics back to food-approved plastic of the other plastic types. Few examples of 
other plastic types are seen. A popular example is HDPE milk bottles in the UK (Bottle2Bottle, 2015). 
WRAP is also researching on recycling food grade PP packaging (WRAP, 2010). In Denmark it is hard to 
imagine a homogenous fraction which is large enough to make a product like PET bottles with similar 
properties, according to Færch Plast. Analysing Figure 7.1 above it was found that the largest homogenous 
fractions within food packaging are LDPE, HDPE and PP. There is potentials in looking at these fractions. 
7.2.2. Demands to processes  
In this section the following questions will be answered for each process in the plastic cycle:  
 
What demands is there to the process in order to produce high quality? 
 
What is possible currently, and what is missing in order to achieve higher quality?  
 
What is currently done in this life cycle stage to improve quality, and what can be done for further 
improvement? 
The four limitations categories (polymer cross contamination, additives, impurities and degradation) will 
be in focus, as there is knowledge gaps between limitations and properties. Therefore, properties cannot 
be evaluated, even as they are determining for the quality in the end. The figures in the following 
sections refer to the step(s) in the value chain, sketched in Figure 7.2. The sections to follow are based on 
knowledge from especially Chapter 2 and 6 and references cited within these chapters. 
==PAGE:70==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 56 of 110 
 
FIGURE 7.2. THE PATHWAYS FOR THE THREE QUALITIES OF 
RECYCLED PLASTIC IN THE HOUSEHOLD PLASTIC VALUE CHAIN. 
This section leads to the calculation of the current, possible and future high quality recycling possibilities. 
The traffic light will be used to illustrate the result of three categories of quality in recycling; red, orange 
and green, sketched in Figure 7.2. They have been defined for simplicity reasons. The quality categories 
are similar to the qualities defined in Figure 6.2. The green category represents a closed loop recycling 
system within a product category with high quality. The orange category represents recycled plastic, 
which replace virgin plastic in similar or other product categories, both inside and outside the household 
products. The plastic is of medium quality, and will not be able to replace food packaging but instead 
assumingly half of the potential of the other commodity groups, i.e. the consumption. The red category 
represents the low quality recycling e.g. polymer blends. The recycled plastic replace other materials, 
substitute low quality products (often outside the household plastic) or are supplied to non-existing needs.  
7.2.2.1. 
Upgrading 
Analysing the needs for high quality recycling, a number of requirements are 
found necessary of the upgrading process. Additionally, in order to upgrade the 
polymers some conditions for the incoming material must be fulfilled. The limits 
of contamination were estimated in Figure 6.2. Analysing Chapter 2 it is found 
that it is currently possible to improve certain properties of plastics, including 
loss of mechanical properties, degradation and lack of stabilisers, i.e. what 
cannot be improved need to be handled in earlier processes. There is a need to 
test the input material, so the right processes can be applied in order to upgrade the plastic to the 
necessary standard. Impact modification is necessary as well as re-colouring and re-stabilisation, so 
further degradation is avoided. Melt filters after extrusion have to sort out what has not been removed in 
the previous processes. Degradation need to be determined in order to restore polymer length improving 
the properties. Fillers and compatibilisation should be avoided where possible in the upgrading, as the 
chance of re-recycling the products will decrease. The input material might at times be of such a low 
quality that compatibilisation and fillers is a necessity to make a useful end product. Fillers affect thermal 
expansion coefficient, which means the flexibility will change and also the plastic cannot be recycled 
==PAGE:71==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 57 of 110 
again. Other additives which imply the plastic cannot be recycled again also need to be avoided. 
Improving mechanical properties, dealing with degradation, re-stabilisation, re-colouring and impact 
modification are all processes which are possible to perform today. Still almost none of the household 
products defined in Figure 7.1 are currently being replaced by recycled household plastics. This is not an 
expression of what is possible with current recycling technology, but due to slow development in the 
recycling sector compared to the plastic production and due to the heterogeneous input material and 
costs of upgrading. Experiences with post-industrial waste are transferred to post-consumer waste, where 
waste streams are more heterogeneous and the need to upgrade greater. Nevertheless, it must be realised 
the needs for upgrading are different due to a difference in input. Currently, upgrading the polymers by 
impact modification and reactive repair modules is not practiced widely in recycling industry. This is 
possible, but is not done, due to unknown reasons. Possible reasons could be low knowledge in the 
recycling sector and economics could also play a role.  
7.2.2.2. 
Sorting 
The sorting process ought to be able to sort into single polymer streams, food 
grade plastics, colours and sort into similar properties (as suggested by Figure 
7.1) to meet the demands of the upgrading process to produce a high quality 
outcome. The plastic polymer streams with similar property requirements need to 
be determined, but Figure 7.1 gives an indication of commodities with similar 
properties. The plastic grades need to be processed individually to avoid cross 
contamination of other plastics, additive combinations mixing, and food approved 
plastics with not food approved plastic. The recycling system should be flexible, as the demand of plastic 
is continuously changing. Sorting the colours or removing them also make the re-colouring easier making 
the recycled plastic reach a larger application range. All non-plastic impurities should be sorted or washed 
out, and the food approved plastic should be decontaminated.  
It is not currently possible to sort after food grade plastic or after properties. The similar properties could 
be sorted after the product type, as PET bottles are already. Here therefore lies a great challenge if 
recycling to higher quality products should be achievable. Sorting food grade plastics would require a type 
of identification system. Currently all plastics and other materials intended for food use have to be 
marked with the food safe symbol. Therefore changing the symbol to something, which can be recognised 
by sorting technology, might be an option to separate the food grade plastics from the plastic stream. 
WRAP researched on five methods; “Injection moulding a grating on the packaging surface; Impression 
moulding a grating on the packaging surface; Printing a grating on a packaging label; Holographic film 
containing a grating, applied to a packaging label; and Laser engraving a grating on the packaging 
surface.” and current work suggest that the holographic film performs best (McKinlay et al., 2013, p. 1).  
Sorting into plastic types and colours is possible, as well as sorting/washing out impurities. At the moment 
the most widespread sorting technology in Europe is NIR spectroscopy (Bakker et al., 2009). There are still 
some difficulties with sorting the plastic into polymers of black coloured products. Currently many grades 
on the recycling market are blends of multiple polymers. The sorting efficiency depends on the incoming 
material and technology, but can reach at least 85% and purity of the outsorted material of at least 90% 
according to Steinert Global with NIR spectroscopy with hyper spectral imaging technology [HIS] flake 
sorting (Steinert Global, 2015). New sorting technologies, including Raman spectroscopy is on the rise 
(Langhals et al., 2014). It is furthermore possible to sort other plastics than the five commonly sorted 
today. Currently, sorting happens primarily of the five common plastic polymers with low sorting 
efficiencies as described in Chapter 5 on recycling efficiency scenarios. It is observed in Figure 7.1, that 
plastic types other than PET, HDPE, LDPE, PP and PS constitute 12% of the consumption, and will currently 
not be sorted out at standard sorting facilities. Identification of other plastic types in order to determine 
if any additional plastic types could be mechanically recycled is missing. Among the 15,000 plastic types 
==PAGE:72==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 58 of 110 
some potential candidates in household waste are ABS, ASA, SAN, PMMA, PA, melamine or PCS. The 
fraction quantity is also to be considered, as small quantities would not be viable to recycle. 
7.2.2.3. 
Collection, segregation and use 
The sorting process would be easier and cheaper both in terms of economy and 
environment if the collection scheme focused on the recyclable fractions. There 
is of cause a limit to what citizens can do, but looking at the four main limitations 
to recycling the citizens should be able to exclude non-plastic impurities. 
Furthermore, in order to ease sorting for the citizen labeling plastic which is 
recyclable, e.g. by using the triangular symbol, (Table 2.1), would be an ideal 
way to easily communicate which plastics can be recycled. The following must be 
explicitly mentioned not to be included in the sorting scheme as they are detrimental to the recycling 
process; PVC, PETG, PA as well as PLA and other degradable plastics. Currently, to the knowledge of the 
author municipalities do not base their segregation criteria on technical knowledge becuase the 
availability of knowledge is limited as shown by this thesis.  
7.2.2.4. 
Manufacture, production and extraction 
Improvement in the manufacture and production is possible by changing the 
additives to types which are compatible with the polymer or easily removable. 
The European PET Bottle Platform e.g. has many alternatives for barrier agents, 
which are more or less compatible. Another example is a report from WRAP 
(Moody et al., 2010) that investigates the possibility to have a removable ink 
system and concludes the trials on PET were successful and was approved for 
indirect contact in food packaging. There is a slight increase in environmental 
impact from the recycling, but this is evaluated to be outweighed by increased recycling and quality 
(Moody et al., 2010). Currently it is not possible to do any improvements in the manufacture, production 
and extraction processes. There is low knowledge due to concealment regarding the plastic content and 
product declarations are not mandatory. In Europe, not even the triangular symbol defining the polymer is 
mandatory, which still only indicates the polymer, even when up to 70% of the plastic can be non-
polymeric content, e.g. additives and fillers.  
7.2.3. Results and discussion 
The current, possible, and future opportunities to improve quality in mechanical recycling of household 
plastic are described quantitatively here. A qualitatively evaluation was presented in the previous 
sections. The calculation method will be described first and hereafter the quality improvements are 
described and discussed. Lastly, a quality hierarchy of plastic types and commodities has been calculated 
for the purpose of guiding municipalities.  
7.2.3.1. Calculation method and assumptions 
Quality is quantified as the amount of plastic in a grade which can replace virgin plastic in the same 
commodity group. The plastic grades are defined in Figure 7.1 and consist of 24 grades of plastic: 
 
6 plastic types (PET, HDPE, LDPE, PP, PS, other) and 
 
4 commodity groups (food packaging, chemical packaging, other packaging, non-packaging) 
Three time periods were evaluated: (i) the “current” practice of recycling, (ii) the “possible” today if 
available technology were deployed, and (iii) “future” which considers all the changes suggested in 7.2.2. 
Three qualities, as defined in Figure 6.2, were evaluated. Quality is, as mentioned earlier, calculated on 
the basis of the four limitations of contamination instead of properties - as the knowledge of property 
losses are too weak. The four limitations categories were evaluated on the cause of contamination. Four 
==PAGE:73==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 59 of 110 
possible causes were evaluated, as seen below. The quality of the virgin plastic has not been evaluated, 
except for the assumed amount of additives depending on the commodity group. Additionally, possible 
interdependency of the causes of contamination might exist. This leads to the following parameters which 
all plastic grades have been evaluated on to estimate the quality: 
 
9 systems 
o 
3 time levels (current, possible, future – A, B, C) 
o 
3 qualities (high, medium, low – 1, 2, 3) 
 
4 contaminations categories (polymer cross contamination, additives, non-plastic impurities, 
degradation – i, ii, iii, vi) 
 
4 causes or improvement of contamination (commodity, polymer dependence, previous treatment, 
upgrading – a, b, c, d) 
This results in 144 combinations of parameters that have been evaluated for each grade. Each parameter 
has been given a value of 0 to 100% estimating the amount of recycled material that can achieve 
high/medium/low quality. All percentages are assigned based on the assumption that all plastic waste is 
collected and sent for mechanical recycling, i.e. this is the maximum potential. In total 3,456 numbers 
are behind the calculations; hence the assumption for each parameter of all grades cannot be described 
here. Instead, Table 7.3 presents an example of the calculation of PS food packaging in a future high 
quality system, and the assumptions for each number are presented below, in Table 7.4. Further 
information on the other grades of plastic can be found in Appendix E. The contamination categories and 
their causes are weighted equally, i.e. they are multiplied together with no weighing. This might not be 
the case in reality, but a comparison of the four contamination categories was not possible due to the 
limited information on the quantitative quality losses in each category of properties. It must be noted that 
the values are rough estimations in order to be able to prioritize the plastic grades for recycling. The 
calculations are based on the available literature as described in previous chapters, which has many 
knowledge gaps. Where no knowledge was present educated estimates were made.

a.iii.

d.vi. 
It is assumed that 90% of the incoming material can be upgraded to acceptable level.

by low quality. The results are presented in Table 7.6.

7.2.3.2. Improvement of quality 
The three categories of quality have been evaluated across the three time levels and the results can be 
observed in Table 7.5. The resulting percentage is the percent in mass of the total consumption which can 
technically be recycled. However the medium and low qualities can only substitute the virgin plastic with 
a lower market substitution ratio. The red category can practically not cover the need of household 
plastic products because the quality is too low.  
TABLE 7.5. TOTAL WEIGHTED MASS SUM OF ALL PLASTIC GRADES OF PRESENT, POSSIBLE AND FUTURE RECYCLING 
POTENTIALS IN THREE QUALITIES. 
 
In total 38% of the potential could, from a technical perspective with the means described in 7.2.2, be 
recycled into high quality products. It was estimated that only 0.4% of the potential, i.e. the total 
consumption, is being currently recycled into high quality products. There is therefore a large potential 
for improvement. The main quality losses today occur in the processes previous to the upgrading processes

in polymer cross contamination, additives and non-plastic impurities, as the upgrading process 
performance depend upon the input material. Lack of degradation improvement, i.e. losses of quality, 
happens mainly in the upgrading process because upgrading of degraded polymers does not occur to a high 
degree today. 
The highest quantities, in Table 7.5, are observed in the low quality. However, recycling high quantities is 
not always the most beneficial solution. If the high quality material is just recycled one more time, in 
quantity (i.e. 76%), this would amount to greater mass recycled compared to recycling low quality plastics 
once (57%). Even when considering that only 38% of the recycled 38% can be recycled again (i.e. 14% of 
the recycled material is recycled a second time), then the amount is 52% (38%+14%), hence almost as 
much of the low quality amount. There is though a limited times of recycling loops possible for a material. 
Furthermore, when investigating how much of the demand for new plastic (the consumption) the recycled 
plastic can replace - when considering the quality - a different picture arises, see Table 7.6. The red 
category does not cover a large fraction of the need in household plastic. It is estimated that 
approximately 5% of the need can be covered by low quality plastic. In addition, it is assumed that the 
medium quality can only cover half of the amount of plastic, which is not for food packaging. Non-food 
plastic covers 44% of the consumption, i.e. medium quality could potentially cover maximum 22% of the 
consumption. Recycling into low qualities is actually postponing the environmental problems, as the waste 
management of these products is uncertain and the created material is not recyclable. 
TABLE 7.6. COVERAGE OF THE ACTUAL HOUSEHOLD PLASTIC DEMANDS OVER THREE QUALITY LEVELS AND TIME 
LEVELS. THE TOTAL POTENTIAL USING ALL QUALITIES IS DISPLAYED IN THE RIGHTMOST COLUMN. 
 
Considering using all qualities it has been estimated that currently Denmark could potentially cover 5% of 
the household plastic market with recycled material. It should be possible to cover 24% if all available 
technology were installed. Lastly, 43% of the household plastic market could potentially in the future 
considering technical possibilities be covered by recycled plastic.  
If all contaminants can be removed sorting into similar property groups might not be necessary, as the 
input material would be more homogenous. The industry could be interested in better recycling, if 
Denmark had producer responsibility and would see a benefit from high quality recycling. This has still not 
been observed in Germany that have had producer responsibility for some time, but the higher recycling 
quantities might be assigned to this. The right form of producer responsibility including quality 
considerations fitting Denmark would have to be investigated further. Legislation is another option to 
achieve the necessary changes. Marking recyclable packaging would help the consumer to choose more 
environmental packaging and ease segregation. This will be discussed further in Chapter 8.

performed based on the calculated amount which can replace virgin materials at a high ratio within the 
same product category. The prioritisation can be seen in Table 7.7. The differences are mainly due to the

C. Future 
38%

Page 63 of 110 
contamination in additives and non-plastic impurities in the food and chemical packaging commodity 
classes and the polymer differences in additives and degradation. Hence, it was chosen to expand the 
food, chemical and other packaging categories to two subcategories, differentiating between 
contaminated and not contaminated with non-plastic impurities. 50% of the food packaging, 20% of the 
chemical packaging, and 20% of other packaging are estimated to be contaminated. Examples are a 
mayonnaise tub, drain cleaner bottle or toothpaste tube respectively. No differences in polymer cross 
contamination depending on neither plastic type nor commodity group was found, hence polymer cross 
contamination is alike in all grades. Approximately the same priority is observed in current and possible 
high quality systems (A1 and B1). The reason is that improvements need to happen in all contamination 
categories in order to obtain high quality. 
The highest scores were found in non-contaminated other packaging of PET, contaminated other 
packaging of PET, and non-contaminated other packaging of HDPE and LDPE. The results have not been 
grouped according to plastic type because the plastics in the different commodities have different 
properties. It is nevertheless noted that PET in general has a higher quality due to lower amounts of 
additives and higher possibilities to improvement of degradation in the upgrading process. Especially the 
contaminated food packaging score lower, mainly due to contamination with organic contaminants and 
barrier agents. There are no indications that recycled packaging have a higher quality than other plastic 
products which some municipalities exclude in segregation guidelines. For example PET of both non-
packaging and food packaging non-contaminated has the same quality value.
