8. Discussion 
The discussion is divided into three subchapters. The first subchapter considers the discussion of the main 
findings, the uncertainties and the knowledge gaps. In the second subchapter a discussion on 
environmental implications are presented and lastly recommendations to municipalities are given. 
8.1. 
Main findings and knowledge gaps 
In this subchapter the main findings will be discussed on a general level considering the first five specific 
objectives described in the introduction (Chapter 1). Secondly, the findings are compared to the current 
knowledge and this thesis’ contribution to the field of quality in plastic recycling is pointed out. Thirdly, 
the weaknesses and strengths of the work are discussed, including uncertainties, methods and 
assumptions. Additionally, implications of the findings on policy and practice changes will be discussed. 
Lastly, alternatives to mechanical recycling of household plastic will be discussed. 
8.1.1. Definition of quality 
Existing quality definitions concern, among other characteristics, economics, contamination levels and 
market coverage. However, all these quality definitions are dependent on the properties of the recycled 
material. Consequently, if there is a great loss of properties the quality is low and this suggests a low 
economic value, a high contamination level and a low market coverage.  The quality definition of this 
current report, found in Chapter 1, is based on the circular economy framework, however if the overall 
goal differ from resource preservation the quality definition might look different. The definition defines 
high quality as secondary material with maintained properties which can replace virgin materials at a high 
substitution ratio. The definition denotes that quality can change, as the need of raw material changes 
depending on the market demands. Making legislation based on a definition that is ever changing is 
challenging. Nevertheless, a definition is important to determine the necessary actions to be set in motion 
by politicians. None of the existing strategies and proposals on European or national plan considered a 
quality definition. A goal which is undefined is hard to reach.  
8.1.2. Quantity targets 
The scenario analysis showed with high probability that the quantitative targets suggested by the 
European Commission may not reachable for household plastic within the time frame. The methodology 
used was simple and can only give indications of possible recycling developments for understanding. The 
scenario analysis did not consider external influences such as development of alternative waste 
management or radical changes in consumption patterns. However, this was also the purpose of the 
analysis. The increase in consumption of plastic goods has so far made the market able to absorb all 
recycled material, even the low qualities. The question is what happens when this tendency stops if the 
suggested amounts by politician are achieved. One might assume that when the markets are saturated 
with low quality material the demand for higher qualities will arise, else the market needs to be helped 
via political initiatives. The potential pitfalls of quantity targets are discussed further in the coming 
sections. 
8.1.3. Technical limitations in upgrading process 
The thesis presents an overview of the contaminants affecting properties in recycled plastic, which has 
not been presented before. The knowledge was pieced together from different literature that either tried 
to give an overview, but didn’t include all contaminations or studied certain issues isolated. The existing 
knowledge is fairly qualitative, hence quantitative knowledge is limited. Knowledge that certain 
contaminants affect e.g. mechanical properties are well known, however, exactly which specific 
==PAGE:80==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 66 of 110 
properties within the category of mechanical properties are affected and to which degree is rarely 
investigated. And the fact that mechanical properties are generalised is also questionable, e.g. a 
contamination might have severe effects on the elastic modulus but not the ultimate strength. 
Furthermore, contaminants are known to affect certain properties; however it has not always been 
investigated if other property groups were affected. The theoretical data gathering was obviously limited 
as not all literature could be investigated.  
Based on the investigated literature it was found that four main groups of contaminants limit recycling: 1) 
polymer cross contamination, 2) additives, 3) non-plastic impurities and 4) degradation. The limit to 
polymer cross contamination of 1% is a qualified estimation, but needs further investigation to be 
credible. Knowledge on other polymers than PET is especially missing. The same goes for the non-plastic 
impurities’ limit of 1,000 ppm. The latter need to be investigated to determine if it is currently 
achievable in the sorting facilities. For the two contamination categories of additives and degradation it 
was not possible to establish quantitative limits. In additives there is a great knowledge gap as the 
addressed additives here are not covering all additives and the effects on properties are unknown. 
Uncertainties of estimated limits are great and only estimated as a general limit to all plastic grades, 
however the limits of contamination is dependent upon polymer and properties needed for the specific 
product. The knowledge gaps on the contaminations effect on specific properties were too great to allow 
determination of limits of property losses. Additionally, no studies investigated combinations of all 
contaminants and the cocktail effect on the properties.  
8.1.4. Improvement of quality 
The assessment of quality improvement showed that polymer cross contamination can be limited via 
better design, segregation guidelines considering recyclability and better sorting of the plastic types. The 
additive contamination cannot be improved in the recycling processes, i.e. there is a need to do 
something in other stages. This was found possible only in the production and manufacturing, where 
additives are initially added. Non-plastic impurities were found to be improvable via the pre-sorting 
process, better segregation at the households and a thought out design of products which the different 
design guidelines, described earlier, considered. Degradation is the only contamination which mainly can 
be improved in the upgrading process. The suggested improvements are considered mainly from a 
technical perspective; hence other parameters might be limiting the potential for change. The technical 
possibilities are anticipated to flourish the next couple of years, as the plastics recycling sector is still 
relatively new and in development. The implications for policy and practice are discussed in 8.1.6.  
8.1.5. Quantification of quality 
If all plastic from households was collected today it was calculated that 0.4% would potentially be high 
quality recycling, however as only 4.6% is recycled an insignificant amount of plastic from households is 
currently recycled into high quality material. It is expected from a quality perspective that it is possible to 
recycle 18% of the household plastic, if all were collected, into high quality with current technical 
solutions. Since the gap between current practice and technically possible is great, other parameters must 
impact the amount of high quality recycling, e.g. technical, social, economical and legislative factors. 
Further research on the matter is seen as important. There is a future large quality improvement 
possibility from 18% to 38%. It was estimated that the combined potential for covering the current demand 
of household plastics was 43%, as seen in Figure 8.1. This considers using some of the medium and lower 
qualities, which are still usable if it cannot be made into high quality material. There is i.e. a gap of 56%, 
which is not technically possible to cover with recycled plastic due to unavoidable quality losses. It would 
be possible to cover 43% of the market demand with recycled material, i.e. similar to the quantity mid-
term target of 45% suggested by the European Commission to be reached in 2020. This would imply that a 
part of the recycled material with the long-term target of 60% in 2025 need to cover demands elsewhere 
==PAGE:81==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 67 of 110 
than in the households. This was out of the scope of this report, but further research on the matter would 
be relevant. 
 
FIGURE 8.1. THE COVERAGE OF THE MARKET FOR HOUSEHOLD PLASTIC USING ALL THE QUALITIES. 
CIRCULAR WOULD INDICATE THAT 100% OF THE MARKET IS COVERED BY RECYCLED MATERIAL. 
There are great uncertainties in these numbers as the potential of recycling have a large error margin. 
However, the necessity for improvement to achieve higher qualities is clear. It is better, considering the 
circular economy framework, to recycle lower amounts into good quality plastic than recycle large 
amounts into poor quality plastic. The low quality plastic cannot meet the material demand because of 
the low market substitution, hence the material do not contribute to a circular material flow. The 
calculations performed are based on contaminants and not on properties, which essentially decides 
quality. The calculations are based on uncertain and qualitative information; hence the uncertainties of 
the values are great. Additionally, the contamination categories and cause of them are weighed equally. 
An example is that non-plastic impurities were found mainly to affect optical properties, hence might 
have a lower importance of the overall quality then e.g. additives. The quality hierarchy, found in Table 
7.7, however is more valid, as a large share of the values is based on the relationship between polymers or 
applications. The choice of the 24 plastic grades can however be discussed, as the grades might have to be 
more detailed if the properties of the grades have to be more similar. Finally, another cause which could 
have been considered is the quality of the virgin material. This could potentially change the picture of 
food packaging, as the additives are more controlled, hence the mixture is better known. The quality 
hierarchy will be discussed further in 8.3. 
8.1.6. Implications for policy and practice 
Responses to water and air pollution started from around the middle of the last century with “end-of-
pipe” solutions, prolonging pipes and smokestacks to dilute the pollution. The development soon dictated 
cleaning technologies for the pollution and from the 1980’ies new technologies with cleaner production 
arose. Waste management is sometimes called “the third pollution” next to water and air pollution. The 
pollution control in waste management have through not progressed from being an end-of-pipe solution, 
which considers a waste product that needs disposal. If we truly want to look at waste as a material 
resource, such as air and water are resources, radical changes are necessary in the waste management 
system. The solutions to the pollution in water and air were not possible to solve at the end-of-the pipe, 
but rather upstream solutions were sought. This is the same challenge applying to the management of 
waste today. However who in the value chain is responsible for improving the quality of the plastic? 
Currently municipalities have the responsibility for the collection and transfer to approved waste 
management companies. Municipalities might be held responsible to meet the current quantity targets, 
considering the collected amounts; however cannot directly affect pre-sorting and sorting efficiencies, 
==PAGE:82==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 68 of 110 
which is part of the newly proposed quantity targets with the change in calculation of recycling efficiency. 
The improvement of quality can neither be isolated to municipalities. The municipalities currently do not 
even benefit from recycling, as the collection and sale of waste is an expense. The benefit instead lies at 
the recycler and the buyer of the recyclate. It is questionable if this is reasonable.  
Changes to improve quality could occur with e.g. consumer demand, legislation or industry desiring a 
“greener” image of plastic. Initiatives have already started to spread. One example is the Danish chain of 
supermarkets Coop Danmark that have started a process to label packaging to ease segregation for the 
consumer (Grundtvig, 2015). This is already widespread in Sweden and Norway. Recyclability of the 
packaging is though not considered in the initiative and the labelling would need to consider the 98 
municipalities’ independent segregation guidelines. The suggestion to make the triangular symbol (Table 
2.1) mandatory also need to consider recyclability, as the symbol currently only determines that a plastic 
it made from a certain polymer. Hence the plastic could be unrecyclable. Another example on recent 
initiatives is removal of the legal barrier for companies to take back own packaging (DAKOFA, 2014b), 
which some companies have wished to do for some time, e.g. IKEA and Nespresso. This shows willingness 
politically and in industry.  
Producer responsibility is another possibility. Currently, producers and manufactures see no benefit from 
making recyclable products. Neither does the recyclers (at least not an immediate one). Manufacturers 
might be interested in making recyclable products as cheaper raw materials is beneficial, but this would 
require take back of own products. On the other hand producers of the plastic material would not benefit 
from increased recycling. The question on logistics is also important, e.g. the demand of a constant input, 
both in mass and quality, to a manufacturer is difficult to fulfil by the recycling sector, because their 
input is insecure.  
A more homogenous material would improve quality as the recycling process would become easier. One 
option could be to limit the number of polymers. The five common polymers could potentially be used in 
some of the applications, which are currently of different polymers (12% of the consumption). Further 
research of the property requirements would be needed. Simplifying the additives would also give a more 
homogenous input and result in less sorting needs. It seems there is a great interest in opening a waste 
sorting facility in Denmark (DAKOFA, 2015c). In regard to plastic this would be beneficial as the leftover 
from sorting, the mixed plastic, would be incinerated in Denmark. Environmentally this would be 
beneficial as district heating is needed in the cold northern parts of Europe, but not in the warmer south.  
How the quantification of quality in recycling could be implemented in legislative targets are also worth 
considering. The calculations performed in this thesis are not directly transferable, as they would require 
too excessively work to be demanded in legislation. As suggested in Chapter 5, the following calculation of 
recycling efficiency are to be considered: 
titution
MarketSubs
Sorting
Collection
cycling







Re
 
The question remains how to calculate market substitution in a simple way which considers the quality of 
the output. It could be relevant to consider measuring the four contamination categories or define 
different quality levels depending upon limits of these contaminants. 
8.1.7. Alternatives to mechanical recycling 
A combination of reducing, reusing and recycling might be necessary to achieve a more sustainable plastic 
waste management in compliance with the circular economy framework. Efforts toward reducing and 
reusing in the municipalities today are not accounted for like recycling efficiency, hence striving for these 
alternatives for the municipalities are not directly beneficial, even if they according to the waste 
==PAGE:83==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 69 of 110 
hierarchy are more environmental beneficial. It might even become harder to reach the recycling goals if 
the plastic which are reduced or reused would alternatively be easy to collect and recycle, even when this 
would likely be more sustainable. The new waste prevention strategy by the Danish government 
(Regeringen, 2015), at the time of finalisation of this thesis still in hearing, indicate that prevention is an 
alternative Denmark is striving for. The strategy contain suggestions regarding plastic packaging. The 
general objective is to reduce the amount of problematic substances to ease reuse and recycling. 
Collaboration across the plastic value chain and closed material loops are among the suggestions 
(Regeringen, 2015). When considering design of packaging, it must be considered that the tendency 
towards decreasing the weight of the packaging intended to reduce environmental impacts might in fact 
increase the amount of additives, i.e. reducing the recyclability of the plastic and potentially negatively 
impact the overall environmental performance. The overall environmental impact is therefore always to 
be considered. Furthermore, when considering reduction of food packaging, waste of food is impossible to 
avoid a discussion on, as a reduction in packaging could increase waste of food and vice versa. Food 
packaging was found to be the largest fraction of household plastic. In Denmark today there is commonly 
no feedback on the amount of waste one produces. Considering that other main commodities like water 
and electricity, the companies have the responsibility of making prevention activities, why should waste 
management companies not be given the same task. If prevention is a target, it should also be considered 
not to oversize the facilities to recycle plastic to avoid a repetition of the oversize of incineration 
Denmark sees today. 
Other alternative recycling possibilities should also be considered. The tendency toward biodegradable 
plastic, detrimental to mechanical recycling, need to be discussed. It is certain that a biological recycling 
of biodegradable plastic would also have issues with polluting substances. Furthermore, chemical 
recycling possibilities are also worth considering.  
8.2. 
Environmental considerations on quality improvement 
The main question to be answered in this subchapter is whether changes in the plastic system for 
increasing material quality would influence the current key parameters affecting environmental impacts 
of recycling. This addresses objective number six. Existing life cycle assessments [LCA] will first be 
described followed by implications for the environment considering the future changes to improve quality 
suggested in Chapter 7. Lastly, the environmental potential for high quality plastic recycling is considered. 
8.2.1. Existing life cycle assessments  
A number of LCAs looking at waste management of plastics have been performed recently. Contributing 
with another LCA was therefore not the aim of this study. Instead, the main conclusions from two 
comparisons of LCAs are described here. Comparing LCAs can be difficult due to large differences in 
assumptions and boundaries, hence they have not compared the direct results, but the relative 
(percentage) difference between different waste management options.   
A comparison of ten selected high quality LCAs by Lazarevic et al. (2010) of post-consumer plastics in 
Europe concluded that mechanical recycling is preferred over incineration with energy recovery in the 
investigated environmental impact categories. This positive environmental performance of recycling 
requires the collected plastic to be clean with low contamination of organics for the substitution rate to 
be close to 1:1. The environmental impact categories cover residual solid waste destined for landfill, 
energy use, abiotic resource depletion potential, eutrophication potential, acidification and global 
warming potential. The majority of scenarios favoured mechanical recycling with a relative difference of 0 
to 75% between incineration and mechanical recycling. 
Another comparison of LCAs was performed by WRAP (Michaud et al., 2010). It compares eight LCA studies 
of waste management of plastic waste. The overall conclusion is that mechanical recycling performs best 
==PAGE:84==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 70 of 110 
in regards to climate change, energy consumption, depletion of abiotic resources and water consumption, 
i.e. agreeing to the findings by Lazarevic and colleagues (2010). The comparison also concludes that 
collection of high quality plastics and a substitution coefficient of close to 1:1 are essential. In climate 
change mechanical recycling performs 150% better or more in 63% of the cases compared to other 
managements options. However, all cases of incineration have lower energy efficiencies than what is seen 
in Denmark. Three cases attributed an advantage to incineration; two of these explained that the used 
sorting and recycling technologies required more energy (Michaud et al., 2010). According to Michaud and 
co-workers (2010, p.48) key parameters regarding waste management systems of plastic are: 
 
“the time perspective taken into account for the plastics degradation in landfills 
 
the avoided material production and substitution ratio 
 
the type of energy recovery 
 
the efficiency of the sorting process and the “default” disposal option” 
The first parameter is not relevant for comparing mechanical recycling to incineration, which is the most 
prevailing alternative treatment in Denmark. Both aforementioned reports stated that a high market 
substitution ratio is important to environmental performance. As the market substitution ratio depends on 
the quality of the recycled material, high quality is very important for environmental benefits. This 
parameter will be described in detail later. The third parameter, energy recovery, concerns mainly to 
incineration and can offset the impacts from the waste treatment processes; it is therefore important to 
investigate. The energy conversion efficiency of the incinerator determines first the amount of avoided 
electricity or heat and secondly the choice of substituted power or heat. The latter depends on the 
country  and area considered (Michaud et al., 2010). In Denmark, incineration in general has high 
efficiencies. The marginal electricity and heat is important. The results would also depend upon the 
chosen LCA methodology for allocation and energy substitution. The electricity mix is different from other 
European countries with a large share of wind power. The marginal heat is more locally determined 
because of the limited transportation distance. The results of these LCAs, which are from other countries, 
could look different if the boundary had been within Denmark. Lastly, the sorting efficiency, aka technical 
substitution, is important to the environmental performance of mechanical recycling. 
8.2.1.1. Market substitution 
Avoided material production is highlighted by Michaud et al. (2010) to be the primary reason for 
environmental benefits in mechanical recycling. Consequently, good quality plastics that can replace 
virgin plastics on a high ratio (1 to 1) should be recovered to maximise the environmental performance. 
Shonfield (2008), one of the studies addressed by Michaud et al. (2010), investigated substitution with 
other materials for two recycling scenarios. The base scenario with replacing virgin plastic with recycled 
plastic in a 1:1 substitution, was compared to a scenario where only 20% of the recyclate replaced virgin 
plastic (the remainder was split evenly between substituting for concrete and wood). This is relevant 
today since applications like PP poles, PS garden furniture and PS roofing tiles can potentially replace 
other non-plastic materials (see Table 2.1). Consequently, the global warming potential changed from 
savings to net emissions, i.e. the impacts from the recycling process were larger than the benefit from the 
avoided material production. In addition to the choice of the substituted material, the question that also 
arises is what the ratio of substitution is. Most of current LCAs assume that recycled plastic will substitute 
virgin plastic on a 1:1 basis (Michaud et al., 2010). However, this implies that high quality recycled 
products are obtained. It was found in Chapter 7 that this happens today with only 0.4% of the household 
plastic waste. Market substitution ratios, if present, were found in Table 6.1 to be economically based. In 
addition, Shonfield (2008) demonstrated that a ratio of 1:0.7 was about the breakeven point at which 
recycling and solid recovered fuel [SRF] for cement kilns (assumed comparable to Danish combined heat 
and power [CHP] incineration) were environmentally equal, but that the figure is uncertain and is 
==PAGE:85==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 71 of 110 
different for individual environmental impact categories. Hence recycling plastics which have a lower 
substitution than 70% is not always environmentally beneficial.  
8.2.2. Changes with increasing quality 
Considering the necessary changes found in Chapter 7 for improving quality of recycled plastic, this 
section will study the possible changes to the system and the effect on the environment. It will be 
discussed if the changes in the system could potentially change the key environmental parameters. 
Hereunder in FIGURE 8.2 possible changes in the value chain are postulated. Further research in the field 
would be necessary to draw conclusions. The sorting and upgrading step is further explained below. 
Lastly, a discussion of other implications, which might affect the environmental performance in the 
future, is presented.

FIGURE 8.2. ENVIRONMENTAL IMPLICATIONS DUE TO THE CHANGES IN THE PLASTIC VALUE CHAIN WITH INCREASED

8.2.2.1. Sorting and upgrading 
It was concluded in Chapter 7 that primarily further sorting and improved modification of degraded 
polymers would be necessary to increase the quality in the recycling steps. Lower sorting efficiencies 
might imply a higher quality outcome (lower polymer cross contamination and non-plastic impurities) 
transferred to the upgrading process creating a higher quality final product. Hence it is noted that the 
technical and market substitution is not interdependent. 
Shonfield (2008) found that incineration performed better than mechanical recycling only when there was 
increased energy demand in the sorting and recycling processes. A possible connection with the quality of 
the output was not addressed. Differently, the recycling facility Interseroh, a part of the ALBA group, had 
==PAGE:86==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 72 of 110 
Fraunhofer Institute UMSICHT (Interseroh, 2015a) compare the savings in terms of energy and greenhouse 
gas [GHG] emissions of two different qualities of their recycled material. The higher quality materials save 
less greenhouse gasses (39%) and use less primary energy (17%) compared to the lower quality. The facility 
processes HDPE, LDPE and PP from both industrial and household sources. The greenhouse gas emissions 
and energy savings are displayed in Appendix F. A PET bottle sorting plant with 23 process steps including 
several separation steps, shredding, washing, drying and pelletizing was investigated by Nakatani and 
Hirao (2011). The processes that consume most electricity are respectively pelletizing, crashing, shredding 
and bale opening – see Appendix F. Adding the 12 separation steps’ electricity consumption together do 
not add up to any of the beforehand mentioned processes (Nakatani and Hirao, 2011). Additional 
impurities sorting would seemingly not require vast amounts of energy. 
8.2.2.2. Other implications 
The alternative waste management would also change in the future when quality should be improved. 
Incineration will become less environmentally beneficial in the future, as the substituted marginal energy 
in 2035 would be renewable sources, if the national energy target is fulfilled. 
Furthermore, improvements still need to consider risks of health with using recycled materials for e.g. 
food packaging. Further research would be necessary to understand the consequences of using recycled 
plastic in certain applications. 
Re-recyclability is also to be considered, i.e. the number of cycles a material can go through the plastic 
circle. Increasing the quality of the recycled material would increase the chances that the new product is 
recycled again. This would often not accounted for in current comparative LCA of plastic waste 
management systems, but indirectly with the market substitution ratio. Modelling generally happens by a 
broken recycling circle, aka cut-off method, but it is questionable where the circle should start and end. 
One common method is to attribute the recycling process to the new material produced from recycled 
material. Another method is the open-loop allocation where the impacts of virgin extraction, production, 
recovery and final disposal are shared by all the use-lives of the material. In the cut-off method extraction 
and production are allocated only to the first product. In the open-loop allocation recyclability of the 
product is important to the environmental burden (Franklin Assoiciates, 2011; Strømman, 2010). This 
would be pertinent to investigate further. 
8.2.3. Environmental potential for high quality 
The question left is how much of the household plastic waste generation is possible to recycle from an 
environmental perspective. The limit for when mechanical recycling makes sense environmentally is when 
the sorting and reprocessing of plastic have an environmental load that is less than the one of virgin 
extraction and production. The following will not consider the environmental benefits from incineration or 
other possible waste management options. This consideration is to discuss the maximum environmental 
potential. There are three overall possibilities for the plastic - displayed in FIGURE 8.3 and described 
below.  
Green: Recycling into high quality products with market substitution coefficients of 1:1. The breakeven 
point between high and lower quality recycling is when recycling requires the same environmental load as 
virgin extraction and production. 
Sorting and reprocessing = 1 (Market substitution coefficient of 1:1)  Virgin extraction and 
production  
This first scenario can essentially only be optional for the 38%, which in the previous chapter was found to 
be technically possible to recycle into high quality in the future. The sorting and reprocessing might 
==PAGE:87==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 73 of 110 
though require so high environmental emissions that not all of these 38% ought to be recycled at high 
qualities considering the environment. 
Orange: Recycling into medium and low quality products with substitution coefficients of 1:x, where x is 
the breakeven point for when recycling makes sense environmentally and 0<x<1. The breakeven point, for 
when recycling is not environmentally liable compared to using virgin materials, would be when  
x (Market substitution coefficient of 1:x) = Sorting and reprocessing / Virgin extraction and production  
The current breakeven point was estimated to be 0.7 by Shonfield (2008). Further research would be 
necessary to estimate this for household plastic in Denmark. 
Red: Other waste management options should be considered when recycling is not environmentally viable 
and the market substitution is 1:y, where y<x. 
 
FIGURE 8.3. PLASTIC’S WASTE MANAGEMENT POSSIBILITIES. THE 
GREEN IS HIGH QUALITY RECYCLING WITH A SUBSTITUTION OF 
1:1. THE ORANGE IS LOWER QUALITY RECYCLING AND THE RED 
INDICATES QUALITIES TOO LOW TO BE MECHANICALLY RECYCLED. 
The colours in FIGURE 8.3 do not completely compare to the colours in Chapter 7 as the quality and 
environmental potentials are different. The environmental limitation is assumingly lower than the quality 
limitation, as it might be possible technically to recycle good quality, but not environmentally beneficial, 
due to increased environmental load in the recycling process. The predominating model today is the 
orange; recycling into lower quality products. However, the substitution might be lower than the 
breakeven point and therefore should rather be incinerated. Further research is necessary in order to 
determine the environmental potential to high quality recycling. 
==PAGE:88==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 74 of 110 
8.3. 
Recommendations for municipalities 
The purpose of this subchapter is to address specific objective number seven discussing the results of this 
master thesis in the context of Danish municipalities. Recommendations will be given with basis in the 
knowledge gained on quality recycling and additionally, the quality hierarchy will be discussed. The 
municipalities are also required to consider other parameters, hence some of these will be discussed 
briefly. Weighing the different factors against each other is subjective and will not be performed in the 
report. The focus will mainly be on environmental considerations. A document with considerations when 
planning waste management of plastic in the municipalities has been compiled and is found in Appendix G. 
Within the jurisdiction of the municipalities, the primary responsibility to improve quality in recycling is to 
properly design collection and segregation schemes so that further processing can be executed 
appropriately. Based on the knowledge obtained the following recommendations from a quality 
perspective to the Danish municipalities on plastic recycling are given. Note that these recommendations 
are given only from current knowledge level, but as the knowledge gap within the field is great, the 
recommendations could change with increased knowledge from further research as well as from 
technological development.  
In the technical limitations to upgrading process, it was found that plastic of the polymers PVC, PA and 
PETG as well as degradable polymers are detrimental to the recycling process. E.g. if the municipality is 
distributing biodegradable bags for collection of organic waste it should be pointed out that these cannot 
be used for plastic collection. There is therefore a need to exclude these types of plastic from the 
segregation guideline. 
The quality hierarchy developed in Chapter 7, indicated that contaminated food and chemical packaging 
were of the lowest quality. It could thereof be considered that these plastics should also be excluded from 
the segregation guideline. The dividing line between contaminated and not contaminated is vague, and 
would have to be investigated further. Technically the plastic need to be cleaned in the washing process, 
which typically, although it varies greatly, is either a water- or alkali-solution at temperatures of 60-80°C 
(European PET Bottle Platform, 2014). There was also found no significant difference in quality between 
packaging and non-packaging. Non-packaging plastic is 15% of the waste generation, but only 8% is made 
from the five common polymers. Considerations of the difficulties of communicating this difference should 
be made. Non-packaging could either be included increasing the possible level of contaminating polymers 
or non-packaging could be excluded consequently decreasing the segregation guidelines coverage of the 
potential. It should also be considered that this product group is assumed to increase in the future as more 
products become obsolete. Consequently, this contradict the recommendation of collecting all plastic 
given by BIO Intelligence Service (Mudgal et al., 2013) which was described in the introduction (Chapter 
1). 
8.3.1. Environmental considerations 
In Denmark, the main alternative waste management option to mechanical recycling is incineration. The 
following gross calorific values, is thereof interesting, as they also seem to differ within the individual 
plastic types, e.g. Table 8.1 shows a large difference for PP. The latter might be explained by a difference 
in additives in the plastic. 
 
 
==PAGE:89==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling

Page 75 of 110

In the LCA comparison by Lazarevic and colleagues (2010) five studies of the total ten investigated the 
plastic types individually and overall concluded there were no major differences between the 
environmental performance. Neither did Michaud et al. (2010) indicate there is a difference between the 
plastic types. Further investigations would have be initiated to conclude difference in the plastic types’ 
environmental performance. 
Michaud et al. (2010) also pointed out that the possible needs for washing or cleaning prior to recycling of 
plastics containing organic contaminants may lead to incineration being environmentally preferable to 
recycling. Frees (2002) and Lazarevic (2010) agrees to this consideration. As the residues, such as shampoo 
or ketchup, are mainly organic, the washing step can give rise to a significant COD (Chemical Oxygen 
Demand) load in wastewater. The energy requirements to treat the COD reduce the environmental 
benefits from recycling. On the contrary, the organic content is an advantage for incineration thanks to 
the heating value of the contaminants (Frees, 2002; Lazarevic et al., 2010; Michaud et al., 2010). 
Consequently, the commodity groups containing organic contamination, mainly food packaging, have 
lower environmental benefit. This supports the consideration to eliminated contaminated food packaging 
from the segregation guidelines. 
8.3.2. Other parameters 
Plastic is by weight one of the smallest waste fractions in Europe (European Environment Agency, 2006). 
This consequently indicate that Danish municipalities cannot generate large enough quantities on their 
own to sell to an international market. Furthermore, sending smaller loads to large facilities abroad the 
efforts to increase quality in the collection might be lost, because it will be mixed with other batches. 
Unless similar initiatives to increase recyclability are taken abroad. Since mechanical recycling requires 
large and constant input there lies a great logistical challenge to increase the overall quality of all plastic 
recycling. This again, supports the idea of a Danish plastic sorting facility. 
Currently, primarily plastic of the polymers PET, HDPE, LDPE, PP and PS  is sorted and reaches the 
upgrading process. Presently it therefore does not seem reasonable to collect other plastic types, since 
the leftover plastic will be utilised abroad when it could be incinerated in Denmark generating electricity 
and district heating. The segregation scheme should therefore include PET, HDPE, LDPE, PP and PS. 
Making the segregation schemes fit the current possibilities would cover 88% of the potential. Of course it 
should be considered that setting the segregation criteria too strict could influence the technological 
development negatively, because all plastics collected will be sortable, and the recycling facilities would 
therefore not be challenged to improve. However, a limited segregation scheme will decrease the 
possibilities of polymer cross contamination with other plastic types. Furthermore, the technical 
substitution was one of the key parameters to environmental performance, hence setting the segregations 
scheme after the possibilities in the sorting facility also improves the environmental performance of the 
recycling system.  
==PAGE:90==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 76 of 110 
Currently there are many different segregation schemes in the municipalities and the two most common, 
to the author’s knowledge, is compared below. The estimations from the total consumption of household 
plastic in Figure 7.1 are used to define the coverage. 
 
Only packaging. Covers 85% of the potential, of which 5% cannot be sorted because the packaging 
is of other polymers than the five sorted. Consequently, the segregation covers 80%. 
 
Rigid plastic. Assuming all LDPE is flexible and all of the other plastic types is rigid, the scheme 
covers only 51% of the potential, of which 12% is other plastics than the five common. The rigid 
plastic segregation scheme hence covers only 39% of the potential. 
Municipalities should hence consider including flexible plastic to cover a larger part of the plastic waste 
generation. LDPE covers 49% of the waste generation, but also PP is known to be flexible plastic, likely 
increasing the flexible plastic amount to above 50% of the potential. The question on whether to 
segregate the flexible and rigid plastic is also relevant. Chemically there is not straight distinction 
between the two, but there could be a difference in the sorting process. Hence if municipalities choose to 
combine all plastics in one container it should be considered that “tricks” for improvement of collection, 
e.g. combining the flexible plastic in one bag, could become a problem in the sorting phase.  
Luijsterburg and Goossens (2014) argues that the choice of collection method does not affect quality of 
the material, based on a comparison of source separated and commingled collection of plastic packaging, 
which showed no difference in four mechanical properties. 
In general 40 percent of the Danes are sometimes, often or always in doubt on how to segregate waste, 
and plastic is the fraction which the greater part of Danes, 33 percent, answer to be the most difficult 
fraction to segregate (Analyse Danmark, 2014). Communication is here the municipalities’ best option for 
improvement. Accepting changes in the collection and segregation scheme is as technology develops also 
something Danish municipalities should do. The concept of “nudging” shows that changes makes people 
aware and this can mean improvements of the collection rates. 
Municipalities should also consider where the waste is generated. The largest product group was food 
packaging, and assumingly with an increasing culture to eat on the go, a large amount of the packaging 
waste is generated outside of the households’ four walls. Hence considering segregated collection in urban 
space could be beneficial.  
 
==PAGE:91==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 77 of 110 
9. Conclusion 
 
This master thesis’ overall objective was to investigate how it is technically feasible to recycle household 
plastic waste in Denmark into high quality secondary raw material in compliance with a long-term goal of 
closing the plastic recycling loop, following political thoughts in circular economy. The following seven 
specific conclusions were established to answer each of the specific objectives stated in the introduction. 
 
Quality definitions in legislation is necessary to improve quality in plastic recycling. 
There are many definitions of quality in plastic recycling in the literature that focus on different 
characteristics basically stemming from an evaluation of the recycled material’s properties. The 
properties should as a minimum concern physical, mechanical, chemical, thermal, weathering and optical 
properties. A definition of quality is important in the determination of actions necessary to improve 
quality in recycled plastic. Currently, several legislative proposals, such as the European Commission’s 
Circular Economy Package, aim to improve quality in mechanical recycling, but do not include a definition 
of quality. If circular economy is the goal, quality needs to be prioritised combined with quantity. The 
quality definition have to include high market substitution, implying maintenance of properties so the 
outcome can replace virgin plastic in similar applications.  
 
Suggested plastic recycling targets in the European Commission’s Circular Economy Package are 
not reachable in Danish households within the timeframes. 
The current recycling rate of household plastic waste is estimated to be 5% in Denmark. In the scenario 
analysis it was found unrealistic that Denmark would be able to fulfil the suggested targets of the Circular 
Economy Package of 45% recycling of plastic packaging in 2020 and 60% in 2025. Furthermore, a new 
calculation method for recycling efficiency was suggested including both collection and sorting efficiency. 
Sorting efficiency is here understood as sorting the non-plastic impurity free material according to plastic 
types. The backcasting scenario that showed how to achieve these targets, assuming the sorting facilities 
can sort 66% and 70% of the incoming plastic material in 2020 and 2025 respectively, requires Denmark to 
have a collection efficiency of 68% in 2020 and 86% in 2025. This collection efficiency is similar to the 
highest efficiencies seen in paper and glass fractions today, which have taken decades to achieve – but 
2020 is only half a decade from now, i.e. it seems unrealistic to reach the quantity targets. The problem 
with the suggested targets is that they do not consider quality and do not comply with the overall goal of 
a more circular economy. 
 
Technical limitations in the recycling process “upgrading” depend upon the contamination of the 
input material, namely four contamination categories: polymer cross contamination, additives, 
non-plastic impurities and degradation.  
The thesis presents an overview of contaminants effecting properties in recycled plastic. The maximum 
amount of contaminations of the input material to the upgrading process to achieve high quality secondary 
raw material was estimated to be 1% polymer cross contamination and 1,000 ppm non-plastic impurities. 
The quantitative limit to additives and degradation was not possible to determine. There are large 
knowledge gaps when it comes to contaminations in the input material and the related property losses in 
the recycled plastic. Hence there are great uncertainties in these estimations. Furthermore, further 
research in the interrelationship between the contaminations to understand cocktail effects is necessary. 
 
 
==PAGE:92==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 78 of 110 
 
To increase quality in recycled material improvements are necessary in the entire value chain. 
The main quality losses from “polymer cross contamination”, “additives” and “non-plastic impurities” 
occur today in processes previously to the upgrading process. Differently quality losses from “degradation” 
can only be improved in the upgrading process and cannot be avoided in previous processes. Upgrading the 
degraded polymers does not happen today although this is possible with current technology, as 
degradation in low quality products is not considered problematic, as these are only recycled once.  
The analysis of the plastic value chain showed that improvements of quality need to take place in the 
entire value chain. Especially considering additives that are not possible to improve in the recycling 
process. The following was found necessary to improve the quality: 1) In the upgrading process there is a 
need to improve impact modification and considerer degradation, 2) The sorting process need to separate 
polymers better, separate food packaging and sort according to properties, 3) Segregation guidelines is 
required only include recyclable plastics, 4) In manufacture and production processes compatible or 
removable additives should only be used, and 5) Proper design could furthermore reduce the need of 
sorting for polymer cross contamination and non-plastic impurities. It is concluded that improvements of 
quality is possible, but that all stages of the life cycle have to be considered and that strong political will 
is necessary. Further research on the means for these changes is necessary. 
 
Quantification of quality was possible and the potential to increase high quality plastic recycling 
is great. 
Quantification of quality could not be performed based on material properties losses, hence the four 
contamination categories (polymer cross contamination, additives, non-plastic impurities and degradation) 
were evaluated instead. The low knowledge in the field results in uncertain conclusions in the 
quantification of quality. The quantification of quality is based on the amount of plastic which is possible 
to recycle into high quality plastics replacing virgin plastic in a product with similar demands of 
properties. It was estimated, based on rough estimations and educated guesses, that 0.4% of the 
household plastic waste would be recycled into high quality secondary raw material today, assuming all 
household plastic was recycled. Considering currently available technology it was estimated that 18% of 
the plastics potentially can be recycled into a quality that can replace virgin raw material with a high 
market substitution. Furthermore, 38% is found to have the potential to be recycled into high quality 
plastic considering the improvements in the whole value chain, described before. A combined scenario 
with high, medium and low quality recycling suggest that the technical limit to cover the household 
plastic consumption with recycled plastic from the household plastic waste is 43%. 
 
Quality improvements potentially have both positive and negative environmental consequences. 
The findings of this study indicate that quality improvements in the recycled material will be beneficial to 
the environment because market substitution, which increases with higher quality, is a key parameter of 
the environmental performance. At the same time, increased consumption of energy and materials in the 
recycling process would have to be included in the evaluation of the environmental performance. Further 
research on the quality improvements’ implications on the overall environmental performance of 
mechanical recycling of plastic is necessary in order to decide upon the environmental limit to high quality 
recycling. 
 
 
==PAGE:93==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 79 of 110 
 
Municipalities need to only consider including recyclable plastics in the segregation schemes. 
Prioritising the grades of plastic according to quality was possible. Based on the quality hierarchy 
developed it was found that the plastic grade, which are most capable of replacing virgin plastic, is non-
contaminated PET of other packaging than food and chemical packaging. The grades that scored lowest 
were food and chemical packaging contaminated with organics and chemical substances respectively. 
Hence these grades should be considered excluded from the segregation guideline, which would also be 
environmentally beneficial due to avoided treatment of organics in the wastewater from the washing 
process. Furthermore, specific polymers have to be avoided in the segregation schemes including PVC, PA, 
PETG and degradable polymers such as PLA, because they are detrimental to the recycling process.

Appendix B: Calculation of recycling 
rates 
B.1. 
Herlev 
Collection rate: 77.86 tonnes of plastic from April 2012 to March 2013 (Herlev Kommune, 2015) 
Plastic waste generation rate: 4,200 households (Herlev Kommune, 2015) * 65 kg/hh/yr (Table 2.5) /1000 
kg/tonne  = 273 tonnes/year 
Collection efficiency: 77.86 t/yr / 273 t/yr= 28.5%

Page 100 of 110 
Appendix E: Calculation of quality  
This appendix contains: 
 
Description of high quality systems, 
 
Description of medium and low quality systems, 
 
The future high quality system (C1) calculations in detail 
 
All values for all systems 
E.1. High quality 
Below in each of the time levels the considerations for estimation of high quality percentage in the four 
contamination categories are described.  
E.1.1. Future 
E.1.1.1. Polymer cross contamination 
All grades and polymers have been evaluated equally, as there is uncertainties if the higher demands for 
PET is due to higher value or that PET is more sensible towards polymer cross contamination. Therefore 
95% is the amount assumed able to be recovered for high quality. The losses are due to inabilities is 
previous sorting to sort to the required purity. 
E.1.1.2. Additives 
The products used for food because the barrier agents for food and beverages is assumed higher. 
Additionally packaging used for chemicals are assumed to have higher amounts of barrier agents, which is 
a limitation to recycling. These two commodity categories is therefore assumed to achieve high quality in 
80% of cases. Furthermore, the amount of additives depend on the polymer, so in addition the following 
values are used: 
 
PET - 95%,  
 
HDPE, LDPE and PP - 90% and 
 
PS and other polymers 80%.  
It is assumed additives which are not compatible with recycling is faced out, and the amount are 
decreased. 
E.1.1.3. Non-plastic impurities  
Non-plastic impurities does not depend on polymer, but on commodity group. Food packaging has been 
given 70% as it is assumed to be more contaminated with organics and e.g. labels. Chemicals have 
similarly been given 80%, and other packaging and non-packaging 90%. Furthermore, there is a general loss 
of quality in previous processes, as the sorting perfect, which give pre-treatment a value of 95%, which is 
the assumed amount that can be sorted to the right level of impurities. 
E.1.1.4. Degradation 
The polymer type is important, and assumed to be  
 
PET - 95%, 
 
HDPE, LDPE, PS and other polymers - 90% and 
 
PP - 80%.  
==PAGE:115==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 101 of 110 
Non-packaging products additionally have 80% because life expectancy is higher and the exposure to 
environment greater. 
E.1.2. Current 
The same considerations for the commodities and the polymers are valid as with the future system. The 
polymer cross contamination have losses in the previous processes because of failure to sort the polymers 
from each other, 10% is assumed to be able to sort into the right purity (>1%). Other polymers than the 
five common cannot be sorted, giving other polymers a value of 0%. Losses of quality due to additives are 
caused by need of additives in food and chemical packaging, but also other commodities. Furthermore, 
the additive amount depend on the polymer and the previous processes are not capable of sorting 
additives. Non-plastic contamination stem from all commodity groups, 70% is assumed to be capable of 
sorting to the excepted limit. Additionally, there is a low degree of upgrading degraded polymers, only 
20% is expected to upgrade polymer length today. 
E.1.3. Possible 
The same considerations for the commodities and the polymers are valid as with the future system. 
Compared to presently performed, the possible could improve the sorting of plastic into polymers, to 80%, 
and improve the non-plastic sorting, to 90%. Other polymers can also be sorted than the five common. The 
contamination of additives remain at 60% 
E.2. Medium and low quality 
E.2.1. Current 
Commodities: These are higher than for the high quality, 10% more in all contaminations for medium 
quality and 100% in all categories for the low quality. 
Dependence on polymers is assumed the same everywhere. 
Pretreatment: Compared to the high quality more plastic can reach the lower limit of sorting, in the 
medium it is assumed 38.5% of the common five polymers can be sorted (the average used in Chapter 5). 
Still nothing of the other polymers is possible to sort today. In the low quality everything is used, hence 
the amount which cannot be sorted is used as mixed plastic.The limit to acceptable limit of additives is 
higher in medium and low quality, so a larger amount can pass, 80% is assumed for medium quality. 
Because of higher acceptance of impurities, 90% is assumed for medium quality. For low quality all 
contamination groups is assumed 100%. 
In upgrading process the need of improving degradation is lower, hence more can be accepted, 40 % for 
medium and 60% for low quality. 
E.2.2. Possible 
Comparing to the present, the changes are that 65% can now be sorted into polymers in medium quality. 
Furthermore degradation in upgrading process in introduced and this depend on polymer, so the same 
values as the high quality is assumed; 95% for PET and 90 for the rest of the polymers. In low quality the 
same is true for the degradation. Else everything else is as the present system. 
E.2.3. Future 
In future in the medium system the sorting increases to 95% of bot polymers and impurities. As additives 
are removed and compatible, this increase to 95%. The low quality system in the future is identical to the 
possible.

==PAGE:123==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen 
Page 109 of 110 
Appendix G: Considerations for 
municipalities 
These considerations are intended as an overall guideline to develop a plan for the plastic waste 
management in a municipality. Recommendations from Bigum and Jakobsen (2014b) has also been 
included. Remember a “good” start with collecting only easily sortable and recyclable plastics will 
increase the overall environmental performance. 
G.1. Segregation scheme 
 
To cover most of the potential, the segregation scheme should cover both rigid and flexible 
plastics. 
 
Consider including non-packaging plastic, but also consider that this might increase the amount of 
undesired polymers. 
 
Exclude the polymers PVC, PA and PETG as well as degradable polymers, e.g. PLA. They are 
detrimental to recycling.  
 
Exclude highly contaminated food and chemical packaging. The author personally uses an advice 
to exclude the plastic if the packaging cannot be rinsed with cold water. 
 
Consider recommending sorting after the triangular symbol (only 1, 2, 4, 5 and 6 – not 3 and 7 – 
see Table 2.1) – this could decrease quantity, but potentially increase quality considerably. Also 
remember that the triangular symbol is not a symbol that represent recyclability. 
G.2. Collection 
 
Consider “tricks” that ease the collection might be influential to the efficiency of the further 
processes, e.g. stuffing flexible plastics into a bag/container. 
 
Consider baling could mean a lower sorting efficiency 
 
G.3. Transportation 
 
Consider that a longer transportation could be environmental beneficial if the sorting and 
recycling facility is remarkably better. 
 
G.4. Sorting - criteria for the sorting facility 
 
Sort at least PET, HDPE, LDPE, PP and PS. 
 
Have a sorting efficiency of at least 60% depending on the input and increasing with technological 
development. Remember that the quality of your collection scheme also affect the sorting 
efficiency. 
 
Be aware that mixed plastics is often included in the sorting efficiency, but that this fraction is 
oftentimes used as RDF, e.g. as fuel in cement kilns. It should be clear that the 60% is sorting into 
single polymer plastic streams. 
 
Make sure the purity of the sorted plastic is high, and that a higher purity will increase quality, 
even though it might decrease the quantity sorted. 
 
Other recyclable materials, such as metals and paper, should be sent for recycling 
 
Carbon black plastic should either be sorted out, or the facility should be in the progress of 
research to do so. Black plastics constitute approximately 10% of the plastic in Danish households. 
This fraction can be used as a lower quality product if not sorted into polymers. 
 
A washing step is necessary for household plastic waste – it should be able to dissolve fats. The 
proper treatment of the wastewater is also important for the overall environmental performance. 
==PAGE:124==
Limitations and quality in plastic recycling 
Line Geest Jakobsen

®
procyclen® HDPE BM C2 
 
procyclen® HDPE BM C2 is a blow moulding quality recompound, modified with in-house prepared combination of  
modifiers which assure that during the processing of recycled plastics the melt flow properties can be preserved. 
The material which exhibits improved thermal and mechanical properties can be supplied in a range of colours to  
customer specification and is additionally stabilised.
procyclen® HDPE BM C2 combines an unique set of properties close to the properties of virgin material, with specifically 
adjusted flow, mechanical properties and processability for demanding applications.

®
procyclen® PP 20 QII  
 
procyclen® PP 20 QII is a non-coloured injection moulding quality recompound with modified melt flow rate. Material is charac-
terized by melt flow rate higher than 20 g/10min and adjusted to the requested value of usual injection moulding quality PP.
The material which exhibits improved thermal and mechanical properties can be supplied in a range of colours to customer 
specification and is additionally stabilised.
procyclen® PP 20 QII is a secondary raw material combines an unique set of properties close to the properties of virgin  
material, with specifically adjusted flow  for demanding applications.
Produced with melt filtration at 150-180 µm.

®
procyclen® PP 40 QII 
 
procyclen® PP 40 Q II is a non-coloured injection moulding quality recompound with modified melt flow rate. Material is charac-
terized by melt flow rate higher than 40 g/10min and adjusted to the requested value of usual injection moulding quality PP.
The material which exhibits improved thermal and mechanical properties can be supplied in a range of colours to customer 
specification and is additionally stabilised.
procyclen® PP 40 Q II is a secondary raw material combines an unique set of properties close to the properties of virgin  
material, with specifically adjusted flow  for demanding applications.
Produced with melt filtration at 150-180 µm.

procyclen® PP B12 A

which assure that during the processing of recycled plastics, the melt flow properties can be preserved. 
The material which exhibits improved thermal and mechanical properties can be supplied in a range of colours to customer 
specification and is additionally stabilised.
procyclen® PP B12 A combines an unique set of properties close to the properties of virgin material, with specifically  
adjusted flow, mechanical properties and processability for demanding applications.

recythen
®
recycled-resource
recythen® HDPE 
 
recythen® HDPE is a recycled material from a post consumer recycling process. The raw material is based on HDPE 
and may contain small amounts of other PE types. The recycled material has a very low level of impurities and exhibits 
homogeneous and stable properties. The material can be supplied in various shades of colour on request.
recythen® HDPE is suitable for use and/or admixture in standard injection moulding and extrusion processes and can 
be processed into profiles, piping, sheeting, etc.
Produced with melt filtration at 150-180 µm.  
*Deviation statistically determined.
